Defining the World: The Mutakallimun's Framework
Al-Nasafi defines the world and introduces huduth and qidam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun define the world, its divisions, and the foundational concepts of origination (huduth) and preeternity (qidam).

Core Idea
For the mutakallimun, the world is everything other than God, and its analysis begins with huduth and qidam.

This lesson opens the discussion on the world's createdness. Al-Nasafi begins by setting the agenda: what is the world, what are its parts, and what do we mean by origination and preeternity? The mutakallimun define the world broadly—encompassing all that is not God—and then proceed to analyze its nature. Understanding these definitions is essential before any proof of God's existence can be constructed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation once, noticing the three questions al-Nasafi poses and the key terms introduced.
The passage is cut off, but the main ideas are clear.
2
Grasp the definition of 'world'
Understand that 'world' (al-alam) means everything other than God, including substances and accidents.
This inclusive definition is crucial for the kalam proof.
3
Meet the key terms: huduth and qidam
Learn huduth (origination) and qidam (preeternity) as the two fundamental temporal categories used to classify existence.
These terms recur throughout the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في حدوث العالم: لمعرفة حدوث العالم، يتحدث النسفي مجيبا عن ما هو العالم؟، وما أقسامه؟ وما ماهية كل قسم؟ ثم يعرفنا معنى الحدوث، ومعنى القدم. فيبين أن العالم عند المتكلمين هو ما سوى الله تعالى وقد سمي بذلك لكونه
Discourse on the origination (huduth) of the world: To know the origination of the world, al-Nasafi speaks, answering: What is the world? And what are its divisions? And what is the essence of each division? Then he informs us of the meaning of origination (huduth) and the meaning of preeternity (qidam). He explains that the world according to the mutakallimun (theologians) is everything other than God Almighty, and it was named thus because of its
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage begins the discussion on the world's creation. It frames the inquiry by posing three questions: What is the world? What are its divisions? What is the essence of each division? Then al-Nasafi defines two key terms: huduth (origination, coming into existence after non-existence) and qidam (preeternity, existence without beginning). He states that the world, according to mutakallimun, is everything other than God. The passage is cut off, but it sets up the standard kalam argument for proving God's existence through the contingency of the world.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The world is only the physical universe of bodies.
Correction: The mutakallimun define the world as everything other than God, which includes accidents, substances, bodies, and all created entities.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimun as stated in this passage, what is the definition of the world?
A) The universe of atoms and bodies
B) Everything other than God
C) All that is perceived by the senses
D) Everything that undergoes change

Key Takeaway
The world is everything other than God, and understanding huduth and qidam is the first step in proving its createdness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the world is everything other than God, then what created thing in your life—whether a person, possession, or status—do you secretly treat as if it were uncreated and eternal? Will you repent of that today?
?
Al-Nasafi begins by defining the world before proving its origination. Have you ever seriously questioned your own assumptions about where the world came from, or do you avoid the question because the answer might demand a change in how you live?
?
You now know that huduth means you and everything you love were once nothing. How does that truth pierce your attachment to this world? What one attachment will you surrender to the Creator today because it is only a temporary creation?

Proof that Substances Are Originated from Accidents
The author proves substances are originated from accidents' origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that substances are originated because they are never free from originated accidents, and how this demonstrates the origination of the world.

Core Idea
What cannot be free from the originated must itself be originated.

In this passage, the author presents a foundational kalam argument for the temporal creation of the world. Building on the earlier proof that accidents are originated, he extends the reasoning to substances, showing that since substances always accompany accidents, they too must be created. This completes the case for the world's origination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the provided translation to get the literal meaning.
Notice that the author uses 'substances' and 'entities' interchangeably.
2
Identify the two premises
The argument has two key premises: (1) substances are never free from accidents, and (2) accidents are originated. Identify where each premise appears in the text.
The author states these explicitly.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
From the premises, the conclusion follows: substances are originated. Then, since the world consists of substances and accidents, the whole world is originated. Walk through this reasoning step by step.
This is a classic kalam argument for the temporality of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينتقل بعد ذلك للحديث عن الجواهر، وينتهي إلى أن الجواهر لا تخلو عن الأعراض والأعراض حادثة. إذًا كل ما لا يخلو عن الحادث فهو حادث، الجواهر إذا حادثة أو الأعيان بتعبير آخر. وإذا كان العالم يتكون من الأعراض، والأعيان، وقد ثبت حدوثهما فالعالم إذا حادث.
And after that he moves to discussion about substances, and concludes that substances are never free from accidents, and accidents are originated. Therefore, everything that is never free from the originated is itself originated; thus substances are originated, or the entities in other words. And if the world consists of accidents and entities, and their origination has been established, then the world is originated.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents a key argument for the origination of the world. The author first discusses substances (al-jawahir) and concludes that they are never free from accidents (al-a'rad). Since accidents are proved to be originated, anything that is never free from originated things must itself be originated. Therefore, substances are originated. The terms 'substances' and 'entities' (al-a'yan) are used interchangeably. Since the world is composed of accidents and entities, and both are originated, the world as a whole is originated. This argument is foundational in kalam for proving the temporal creation of the world.

Key Terms
الجواهر
الأعراض
حادث
الأعيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is circular because it assumes accidents are originated without proof.
Correction: The origination of accidents is established in a prior argument (not shown in this passage). This passage builds on that conclusion; it is not circular.

Checkpoint
Why are substances (jawahir) considered originated according to this passage?
A) Because they are eternal
B) Because they are never free from accidents, which are originated
C) Because they are composed of atoms
D) Because they are perceived

Key Takeaway
Substances are originated because they are never free from originated accidents, and since the world is composed of substances and accidents, the world is originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body and everything you own are made of substances that are never free from accidents—change, decay, and dependence. If all these are originated and not eternal, what does that say about your ultimate reliance? Are you living as if you are self-sufficient, or do you truly acknowledge that you are created and sustained moment by moment? What one change can you make today to anchor that reliance in your heart?
?
The passage proves that the world is originated because it is made of originated parts. But you are part of that world. How does the fact that you are an originated being, brought from non-existence, shape your purpose? If you were not always here and will not always be, what should you be doing with your limited time that you are not doing now?
?
You accept that accidents (like health, wealth, relationships) are temporary. But do you live as if they are permanent? Identify one accident you cling to as if it will never leave. How does knowing that every accident is originated and will pass change your attachment to it? What step can you take today to detach your heart from it?

The Contingency Argument for a Maker
The author presents the contingency argument for a maker.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the contingency of the world necessitates a specific determiner (mukhassis) for its existence.

Core Idea
Contingent things do not become actual by themselves; they need an external determiner.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi presents the classic kalam contingency argument. He establishes that the world is originated and therefore contingent, meaning it could equally exist or not exist. Such a thing cannot specify itself into existence; it requires a specifier. This argument is foundational for proving the existence of a creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic source and its translation to grasp the logical flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the key terms ja'iz al-wujud and wajib al-wujud.
2
Identify the key terms
Understand the meanings of 'possible of existence' (ja'iz al-wujud) and 'necessary of existence' (wajib al-wujud) and their implications for the argument.
These terms are central to the contingency distinction.
3
Trace the argument's logic
Follow how the author moves from the world's origination to the need for a specifier. Note the conditional: if it were necessary, it would be eternal; but it is originated, so it is contingent.
The conclusion is that a contingent thing does not actualize by itself.
4
Reflect on the examples
Consider the examples: the world cannot cause itself, and a human cannot change his own blameworthy attribute. These illustrate the need for an external determiner.
Think about why these examples support the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنه قد ثبت حدوث كل العالم، وما ثبت حدوثه يكون جائز الوجود، لأنه لو كان واجب الوجود لكان يستحيل العدم، وكان قديما لكنه قد ثبت حدوثه وما ثبت حدوثه يكون جائزة الوجود، وجائز العدم، وما اتصف بهاتين الحالتين لا يختص بأحديهما إلا بتخصيص مخصص، ويضرب لذلك أمثلة من واقع الحياة ويبين استحالة أن يحدث العالم نفسه بنفسه، أو يحدث نفسه في حالة العدم، ويضرب مثلا من واقع الحياة بكمال الإنسان وسيطرته على الكون، ومع ذلك فهو عاجز عن تغيير صفة له ذميمة.
Indeed, the origination of the entire world has been established, and what is established as originated is possible of existence, because if it were necessary of existence, then non-existence would be impossible for it, and it would be eternal. But its origination has been established, so what is established as originated is possible of existence and possible of non-existence. And what is described by these two states is not specified by either of them except by the specification of a specifier. And he gives examples from real life and shows the impossibility of the world causing itself to come into existence, or coming into existence from non-existence by itself. And he gives an example from real life of the perfection of a human being and his dominance over the universe, yet he is unable to change a blameworthy attribute of himself.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents a classical kalam argument for the existence of a creator, often called the contingency argument (dafil al-imkan). The author states that the origination of the entire world has been established. Something that is originated is 'possible of existence' (ja'iz al-wujud), meaning it is contingent—it could either exist or not exist. If it were necessary of existence (wajib al-wujud), it could not cease to exist and would be eternal. But since it is originated, it is contingent. A contingent thing does not become actual by itself; it requires a specifier (mukhassis) to tilt it from possibility to actuality. The author then gives examples to illustrate: the world cannot create itself, nor can it come into existence from nothing by itself. He uses the analogy of a perfect human who, despite his abilities, cannot change his own bad characteristic, implying that the world's existence requires an external determiner.

Key Terms
جائز الوجود
واجب الوجود
مخصص
حدوث العالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that contingency alone proves the existence of God without further reasoning.
Correction: Contingency shows that the world could not exist by itself, but it requires the additional principle that a contingent thing needs a determiner, and that infinite regress of determiners is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why does the contingent world need a specifier (mukhassis)?
A) Because it is eternal
B) Because it is necessary of existence
C) Because it is equally possible to exist or not exist, so something must tip the balance
D) Because it is perfect

Key Takeaway
The contingency of the world logically demands a specific determiner for its existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that your very existence every moment depends entirely on God's will—that you are contingent—then why do you still live as if you are self-sufficient? Commit today to a specific act that forces you to acknowledge your absolute dependence on Him, such as repeating 'Ya Qayyum' with full presence of heart.
?
When you plan your future, do you honestly feel the contingency of every outcome, or do you assume things will happen by your own power? How would your daily planning change if you internalized that nothing comes to be except by a specifier? Write down one plan you have and explicitly ask Allah to specify it for you.
?
You cannot change a blameworthy attribute of yourself, as the example in the passage illustrates. Is there a sin or character flaw you have been trying to overcome by sheer willpower—and failing? Will you now turn to the Specifier who alone can grant change? What is one concrete supplication you will make starting today?

The Three Paths of Ahl al-Sunnah for Proving the Creator
Al-Shahrastani enumerates three Sunni proofs for the Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three classical Sunni methods for proving the existence of a creator: inference from events, inference from possibility, and the proof of invention.

Core Idea
Ahl al-Sunnah uses three rational paths to prove the Creator.

Al-Shahrastani outlines the methodologies of the mutakallimun. The passage presents two main approaches—from temporal events and from possibility—and adds al-Ash'ari's proof of invention, forming a threefold foundation for Sunni theology. This lesson introduces these methods before diving into Nasafi's specific argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the three methods
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is an enumeration.
2
Identify each method and its rationale
The passage mentions three methods: inference from events, inference from possibility, and proof of invention. For each, note how they lead to a creator.
3
Understand why these methods are foundational
These three methods represent the core of Sunni theology's rational approach to proving God's existence. They complement each other.
They are not contradictory but focus on different aspects of creation.
4
Notice that Nasafi is placed within this tradition
The passage explicitly says that Nasafi's argument falls under these three paths. This contextualizes what follows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحكي الشهرستاني في نهاية الإقدام ص ١٢٤ مسلك أهل السنة والجماعة كما هو مذكور في النص التالي: سلك المتكلمون طريقين في إثبات الصانع الأول الاستدلال بالحوادث على محدث صانع، وسلك الأوائل طريقا آخر، وهو الاستدلال بإمكان الممكنات على مرجّح لأحد طرفي الإمكان - فإذا أضفنا إلى الطريقتين ما قال به الأشعري وهو دليل الاختراع خرجنا بثلاث طرق من طرق أهل السنة والجماعة، يندرج تحتها النسفي حيث يقول: إنه قد ثبت حدوث كل العالم، وما ثبت حدوثه يكون جائز الوجود، لأنه لو كان واجب الوجود لكان يستحيل العدم، وكان قديما لكنه قد ثبت حدوثه وما ثبت حدوثه يكون جائزة الوجود، وجائز العدم، وما اتصف بهاتين الحالتين لا يختص بأحديهما إلا بتخصيص مخصص، ويضرب لذلك أمثلة من واقع الحياة ويبين استحالة أن يحدث العالم نفسه بنفسه، أو يحدث نفسه في حالة العدم، ويضرب مثلا من واقع الحياة بكمال الإنسان. وسيطرته ذلك فهو عاجز عن تغيير صفة له ذميمة. على الكون، ومع ويقول: إن الأعيان في العالم تتكون من طبائع متنافرة، لو تركت وشأنها لتنافرت إذ الاجتماع ليس من قبل ذواتها، وإنما من قبل قادر لا يغالب عزيز لا يمانع وهو في حديثه يبطل قول ثمامة، والعلاف، وبشر بن المعتمر، وابن الراوندي، والكرامية. ويخلص إلى أن وراء الكون إلها أبدعه من العدم، وهو هنا لا يختلف مع الأشاعرة في شيء.
Al-Shahrastani narrates in Nihayat al-Iqdam p. 124 the way of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah as mentioned in the following text: The mutakallimun followed two paths in proving the Maker: first, inferring from events to an event-making maker; and the early ones followed another path, which is inferring from the possibility of possible things to a preponderator for one of the two sides of possibility. So if we add to the two paths what al-Ash'ari said, which is the proof of invention, we come out with three paths from the paths of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, under which al-Nasafi is included, for he says: It has been established that the entire world is originated, and whatever is established to be originated is possible of existence, because if it were necessary of existence, then non-existence would be impossible for it, and it would be eternal. But it has been established that it is originated, and whatever is established to be originated is possible of existence and possible of non-existence. And whatever is described by these two states is not specific to one of them except by the specification of a specifier. And he gives examples from real life and shows the impossibility that the world could bring itself into existence, or bring itself into existence from the state of non-existence, and he gives an example from real life of the perfection of man and his control over the universe, yet he is unable to change a blameworthy attribute of himself. And he says: The entities in the world consist of conflicting natures; if left alone they would conflict, for composition is not from their own essences, but from a Powerful One who cannot be overpowered, a Mighty One who cannot be resisted. And in his discussion he refutes the sayings of Thumama, al-'Allaf, Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir, Ibn al-Rawandi, and the Karramiyya. And he concludes that behind the universe is a God who created it from nothing, and here he does not differ with the Ash'aris in anything.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents a summary of the Sunni proof for the existence of a creator as articulated by al-Nasafi. It begins by citing al-Shahrastani, who states that the mutakallimun employed two main methods: inference from temporal events to a temporal maker, and inference from the possibility of contingent things to a preponderator. Adding al-Ash'ari's proof of invention yields three methods. Al-Nasafi's argument is then given: the entire world is originated; whatever is originated is contingent in existence (ja'iz al-wujud), not necessary, because if it were necessary it would be eternal and impossible to cease. Since it is originated, it is both possible to exist and possible not to exist, and it cannot be specific to one of these states without a specifier (mukhas̄sis). He gives examples showing that the world cannot create itself. He also argues that substances in the world have conflicting natures and would not unite unless compelled by a powerful, irresistible agent. He refutes various opponents (Thumama, al-'Allaf, etc.) and concludes that behind the universe is a God who created it ex nihilo, aligning with the Ash'aris on this point.

Key Terms
الاستدلال بالحوادث
الاستدلال بإمكان الممكنات
دليل الاختراع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Muslim theologians only used one method.
Correction: Actually, they employed multiple rational approaches, including inference from temporal events, from possibility, and the proof of invention.

Checkpoint
How many paths for proving the Maker are mentioned in this passage?
A) Two
B) Three
C) One
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunnah employs three distinct but complementary rational proofs for the existence of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of these three proofs resonates most deeply with your own certainty in Allah's existence? Have you ever relied on only one, and if so, how might that have left you vulnerable when doubts arose? Take a moment to memorize one of these proofs so you can recall it in times of doubt.
?
The passage mentions that these three paths are complementary. In your own life, have you neglected the proof from possibility—the idea that everything contingent needs an external preponderator? How does this truth affect your daily awareness of your utter dependence on Allah? Commit to reflecting on this before you sleep tonight.
?
Al-Ash'ari's proof of invention declares that the world was brought into being from nothing. How does this shape your view of your own existence? Are you living as if you are a created being, accountable to your Creator in every action? Write a short prayer thanking Allah for creating you from nothing and asking for guidance to live accordingly.

The Contingency Argument: The Need for a Specifier
Nasafi states the core contingency argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that because the world is originated and contingent, it requires a specifier to bring it into existence.

Core Idea
What is possible does not exist without a specifier.

Having established the methods, this lesson presents Nasafi's own formulation of the contingency argument. He begins from the established fact that the entire world is originated. From this, he deduces that it is contingent in existence and therefore needs a specifier (mukhasis) to tip the balance from non-existence to existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic argument slowly
Read the passage once through. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow: premise, implication, conclusion.
This is a deductive argument.
2
Identify the premise: the world is originated
The argument starts with the statement that it has been established that the entire world is originated (hadith). This is the foundation.
3
Understand the implication: it is contingent
If it were necessary, it would be eternal. But it is originated, so it is possible (ja'iz) to exist and not exist.
4
Grasp the need for a specifier
Something that is equally possible to exist or not cannot become specific to one state without an external specifier (mukhasis). That specifier is God.
5
Reflect on why this leads to a creator
This specifier must be external and powerful enough to bring the world into existence. This is the core of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحكي الشهرستاني في نهاية الإقدام ص ١٢٤ مسلك أهل السنة والجماعة كما هو مذكور في النص التالي: سلك المتكلمون طريقين في إثبات الصانع الأول الاستدلال بالحوادث على محدث صانع، وسلك الأوائل طريقا آخر، وهو الاستدلال بإمكان الممكنات على مرجّح لأحد طرفي الإمكان - فإذا أضفنا إلى الطريقتين ما قال به الأشعري وهو دليل الاختراع خرجنا بثلاث طرق من طرق أهل السنة والجماعة، يندرج تحتها النسفي حيث يقول: إنه قد ثبت حدوث كل العالم، وما ثبت حدوثه يكون جائز الوجود، لأنه لو كان واجب الوجود لكان يستحيل العدم، وكان قديما لكنه قد ثبت حدوثه وما ثبت حدوثه يكون جائزة الوجود، وجائز العدم، وما اتصف بهاتين الحالتين لا يختص بأحديهما إلا بتخصيص مخصص، ويضرب لذلك أمثلة من واقع الحياة ويبين استحالة أن يحدث العالم نفسه بنفسه، أو يحدث نفسه في حالة العدم، ويضرب مثلا من واقع الحياة بكمال الإنسان. وسيطرته ذلك فهو عاجز عن تغيير صفة له ذميمة. على الكون، ومع ويقول: إن الأعيان في العالم تتكون من طبائع متنافرة، لو تركت وشأنها لتنافرت إذ الاجتماع ليس من قبل ذواتها، وإنما من قبل قادر لا يغالب عزيز لا يمانع وهو في حديثه يبطل قول ثمامة، والعلاف، وبشر بن المعتمر، وابن الراوندي، والكرامية. ويخلص إلى أن وراء الكون إلها أبدعه من العدم، وهو هنا لا يختلف مع الأشاعرة في شيء.
Al-Shahrastani narrates in Nihayat al-Iqdam p. 124 the way of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah as mentioned in the following text: The mutakallimun followed two paths in proving the Maker: first, inferring from events to an event-making maker; and the early ones followed another path, which is inferring from the possibility of possible things to a preponderator for one of the two sides of possibility. So if we add to the two paths what al-Ash'ari said, which is the proof of invention, we come out with three paths from the paths of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, under which al-Nasafi is included, for he says: It has been established that the entire world is originated, and whatever is established to be originated is possible of existence, because if it were necessary of existence, then non-existence would be impossible for it, and it would be eternal. But it has been established that it is originated, and whatever is established to be originated is possible of existence and possible of non-existence. And whatever is described by these two states is not specific to one of them except by the specification of a specifier. And he gives examples from real life and shows the impossibility that the world could bring itself into existence, or bring itself into existence from the state of non-existence, and he gives an example from real life of the perfection of man and his control over the universe, yet he is unable to change a blameworthy attribute of himself. And he says: The entities in the world consist of conflicting natures; if left alone they would conflict, for composition is not from their own essences, but from a Powerful One who cannot be overpowered, a Mighty One who cannot be resisted. And in his discussion he refutes the sayings of Thumama, al-'Allaf, Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir, Ibn al-Rawandi, and the Karramiyya. And he concludes that behind the universe is a God who created it from nothing, and here he does not differ with the Ash'aris in anything.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents a summary of the Sunni proof for the existence of a creator as articulated by al-Nasafi. It begins by citing al-Shahrastani, who states that the mutakallimun employed two main methods: inference from temporal events to a temporal maker, and inference from the possibility of contingent things to a preponderator. Adding al-Ash'ari's proof of invention yields three methods. Al-Nasafi's argument is then given: the entire world is originated; whatever is originated is contingent in existence (ja'iz al-wujud), not necessary, because if it were necessary it would be eternal and impossible to cease. Since it is originated, it is both possible to exist and possible not to exist, and it cannot be specific to one of these states without a specifier (mukhas̄sis). He gives examples showing that the world cannot create itself. He also argues that substances in the world have conflicting natures and would not unite unless compelled by a powerful, irresistible agent. He refutes various opponents (Thumama, al-'Allaf, etc.) and concludes that behind the universe is a God who created it ex nihilo, aligning with the Ash'aris on this point.

Key Terms
حدوث العالم
جائز الوجود
تخصيص مخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think contingent things can come into existence by themselves.
Correction: Nasafi argues that contingent things have equal possibility for existence and non-existence, so they require an external specifier to bring them into existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can't the world be necessary in existence?
A) Because it is eternal.
B) Because it has been established to be originated.
C) Because it is powerful.
D) Because it is composed.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's contingency argument shows that the originated world, being contingent, must have a specifier – the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that every contingent thing needs a specifier to bring it into existence. Think of one area of your life where you have been acting as if your own choices or circumstances are self-sufficient, independent of Allah’s determination. What step can you take today to consciously acknowledge Allah as the Specifier in that area?
?
Nasafi argues that the world cannot specify itself for existence without a Creator. In what ways do you still live as if you are the master of your own existence, forgetting that you are contingent at every moment? Write a specific commitment to remind yourself daily of your dependence on Allah.
?
The passage states that contingent things are equally possible to exist or not exist. How does this truth change the way you view your own successes and failures? Make a concrete intention to thank Allah for the mere fact that He chose to give you existence and every blessing.

Evidence from Nature and Refutation of Opponents
Nasafi provides empirical examples and refutes opponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses examples from human inability and conflicting natures to further support the need for a Creator, and how he refutes the views of several theological opponents.

Core Idea
Creation's complexity and conflict point to a single Powerful Creator.

After presenting the logical argument, Nasafi strengthens his case with tangible examples from life. He points out that humans, despite controlling much of the universe, cannot change even a blameworthy attribute of themselves, showing their limitation. He also notes that the world's elements have conflicting natures that would not unite spontaneously. These observations refute those who deny a Creator or attribute creation to nature or human power.

Guided Path
1
Read the examples given
Read the passage and identify the two main examples: man's inability to change his own trait, and the conflicting natures of entities.
These are not mere analogies but direct evidence.
2
Understand why these examples support the need for a specifier
The examples show that the world's order cannot be self-caused; an external, powerful agent is needed.
3
Identify the opponents refuted
Nasafi refutes Thumama, al-'Allaf, Bishr, Ibn al-Rawandi, and the Karramiyya. Briefly note their positions if known.
Their specific views are not detailed here.
4
Grasp the conclusion that the universe was created ex nihilo by God
The passage ends by affirming that behind the universe is a God who created it from nothing, aligning with Ash'ari theology.

Source Text

Proof of God's Oneness from Mutual Hindrance
Al-Nasafi presents the argument of mutual hindrance to prove God's unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument of mutual hindrance (tamānuʿ) that proves the oneness of the Creator.

Core Idea
Two creators cannot coexist because they would inevitably contradict each other.

The argument of mutual hindrance is a powerful logical proof for the oneness of Allah. Al-Nasafi presents it concisely, showing that if there were two creators, their wills would conflict, leading to an impossibility. This passage demonstrates how reason alone can establish a fundamental theological truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if there were two creators, mutual hindrance would exist.'
2
Understand the assumption
The argument assumes both creators are omnipotent and independent. Consider what happens when one wills a thing to exist and the other wills it not to exist.
3
Follow the logical consequences
Mutual hindrance implies either both are originated (had a beginning) or one is originated. Why? Because a true eternal Creator cannot be hindered.
4
Derive the conclusion
Since the existence of two creators leads to an impossibility (combination of opposites), the only consistent conclusion is that there is only one Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقول : إن هذا الكلام تبرع منه إذ لا حاجة بنا إلى شيء من هذا، لأنه لو كان هناك صانعان لوجد التمانع، والتمانع يدل على حدوثهما ، أو حدوث العاجز منهما، لأنه لو حصل ما يريدانه في الشيء لاجتمع النقيضان وهذا محال إذا الصانع واحد وهو الله.
He says: This speech is a favor from him, for we have no need of any of this, because if there were two creators, mutual hindrance would exist, and mutual hindrance indicates the originatedness of both, or the originatedness of the weaker of them, because if what they both will regarding a thing occurred, the two opposites would combine, and that is impossible. So the Creator is one, and He is Allah.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the oneness of God (tawḥīd) based on the argument of mutual hindrance (tamānuʿ). The author presents a direct refutation of dualism. The argument begins by stating that if there were two creators, then when one wills a thing to exist and the other wills it not to exist, either both wills are realized (which is impossible because existence and non-existence cannot combine) or neither is realized (which indicates weakness). This mutual hindrance proves that both are either originated or one is originated and the other is not. But if one is originated, the other is the true Creator. Therefore, the Creator is one and unique. This argument assumes that both creators are omnipotent and independent.

Key Terms
تمانع
صانع
حدوث
نقيضان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think two gods could cooperate and never disagree.
Correction: The argument shows that even the possibility of disagreement (which is inherent in independence) leads to impossibility. Omnipotence requires ability to will anything, so conflict is inevitable.

Checkpoint
What does the argument of mutual hindrance (tamānuʿ) prove about the possibility of two creators?
A) They could exist if they agree
B) They would both be eternal
C) They would both be originated or one is originated
D) They are both bodies

Key Takeaway
The logical impossibility of two creators proves that the Creator is necessarily one and unique.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that only Allah is the sole Creator, why do you still act as if your own plans or other people's wills can override His? What would change if you lived this belief today?
?
The argument shows that conflicting wills lead to impossibility. Are there areas in your life where you are trying to serve two masters—your desires and Allah’s command? Which master must you choose right now, and what concrete step will you take to align with His will?
?
When you face a situation where two outcomes seem equally possible, do you trust that only Allah’s will prevails, or do you panic as if multiple forces control your fate? How can you train your heart to rest in the oneness of His creative power?

Proof of the Eternity of the Creator
Al-Nasafi argues the Creator's eternity from impossibility of infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi proves the eternity of the Creator by avoiding infinite regress.

Core Idea
The Creator must be eternal to stop an infinite chain of origins.

Al-Nasafi builds on two previously established truths: the world is originated and there is a Creator. From these, he draws a logical conclusion about the Creator's nature. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two premises
Remind yourself that al-Nasafi assumes we already proved the world is originated (ḥādith) and that a Creator (ṣāniʿ) exists.
2
Understand the logical inference
If the Creator were not eternal, He would need another originator, leading to an infinite chain. That is impossible.
3
Grasp the impossibility of infinite regress
An infinite regress (tasalsul) is inconceivable and thus impossible. This forces the first originator to be eternal.
This is a key argument in Islamic theology (kalām).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إننا أثبتنا أن العالم حادث وأثبتنا وجود الصانع، وبثبوت هذين الأمرين ينتج قدم الصانع؛ لأنه إذا لم يكن قديما لافتقر إلى محدث، والآخر إلى آخر إلى ما لا نهاية، وما لا نهاية له لا يُتصور .
We have established that the world is originated and we have established the existence of the Creator, and by the establishment of these two matters, the eternity of the Creator follows; because if He were not eternal, He would need an originator, and that other would need another, ad infinitum, and what has no end is inconceivable.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the eternity (qidam) of God. The author builds on two previously established points: the world is originated (ḥādith) and there is a Creator (ṣāniʿ). From these, he deduces that the Creator must be eternal because if He were originated, He would require another originator, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). An infinite regress is impossible because it is inconceivable and leads to absurdity. Therefore, the first originator must be eternal, without beginning.

Key Terms
قدم
حادث
صانع
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People might think God could be both eternal and originated by another.
Correction: This argument shows that if God were originated, He would not be the first cause, leading to impossible infinite regress.

Checkpoint
Why must the Creator be eternal according to this argument?
A) Because the world is eternal
B) Otherwise He would need an originator, leading to infinite regress
C) Because He is a substance
D) Because accidents cannot be eternal

Key Takeaway
The eternity of the Creator follows logically from the impossibility of an infinite regress of causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument forces you to stop at an eternal Creator. Is there any area of your life where your own reasoning or actions are chasing an infinite regress—like seeking approval, accumulating wealth, or postponing repentance? What would it look like to truly stop at God today and let Him be your final origin?
?
If you fully believed that every good thing in your life ultimately traces back to an eternal, self-sufficient Creator, how would your gratitude or reliance change right now? Name one specific blessing you have taken for granted and commit to seeing it as a direct gift from the Eternal.
?
The proof shows that something must be eternal to make sense of existence. Without God, your own existence would be an impossible infinite chain. How does that affect the way you view your own worth and purpose? Will you live today as if you are held by the Eternal, or as if you are lost in a pointless loop?

5 min
Proof of the Eternity of the Creator
Nasafi proves the Creator's eternity from the origination of the world and the existence of the Maker.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi deduces the eternity of the Creator from two premises: the world is originated and there is a Maker, and why an infinite regress is impossible.

Core Idea
The impossibility of infinite regress demands an eternal Creator.

This lesson examines Nasafi's concise proof for the Creator's eternity. Starting from two established premises—the world is originated and there is a Maker—he argues that the Maker must be eternal to avoid an absurd infinite chain of causes. This is a central argument in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Pay attention to the key terms: قدم, حادث, الصانع.
2
Identify the two premises
Notice that Nasafi begins by stating two things he has already proven: the world is originated (حادث) and there is a Maker (الصانع).
These are the foundation of the argument.
3
Understand the logical deduction
He then says that from these two, the eternity of the Maker follows. The reasoning is: if the Maker were not eternal, He would need an originator, leading to an infinite chain, which is impossible.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Reflect on why infinite regress is impossible
Nasafi states that 'what has no end cannot be conceived'. Reflect on why an infinite chain of causes cannot exist in reality.
Think about logical vs. actual infinity.
5
Conclude the necessity of eternity
Therefore, the chain must stop at an eternal, uncaused being – the Creator.
This conclusion is central to Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قدَمُ الباري عز وجل: يتحدث عن قدم الباري عز وجل فيقول : إننا أثبتنا أن العالم حادث وأثبتنا وجود الصانع، وبثبوت هذين الأمرين ينتج قدم الصانع؛ لأنه إذا لم يكن قديما لافتقر إلى محدث، والآخر إلى آخر إلى ما لا نهاية، وما لا نهاية له لا يُتصور .
The Eternity of the Creator, Mighty and Majestic: He speaks about the eternity of the Creator, Mighty and Majestic, and says: We have proven that the world is originated and we have proven the existence of the Maker, and by the establishment of these two matters the eternity of the Maker follows; because if He were not eternal, He would need an originator, and the other (would need) another, ad infinitum, and what is infinite cannot be conceived.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalil) for the eternity of the Creator (qidam al-Bari). It begins by identifying two premises: first, the world is originated (hadith); second, there exists a Maker (al-Sani'). From these two premises, the conclusion (natija) follows: the Maker must be eternal (qadim). The reasoning is that if the Maker were not eternal, He would Himself require an originator (muhdith). That originator would then require another, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible (la yatasawwar). Therefore, the chain must stop at an Eternal Being. This is a classic kalam argument for the necessity of an uncreated First Cause.

Key Terms
قدم
حادث
الصانع
محدث
ما لا نهاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the proof assumes the existence of the Maker arbitrarily, rather than deriving it from the world's contingency.
Correction: Nasafi explicitly states that the existence of the Maker is proven elsewhere (not in this passage). This proof only combines that with the origination of the world to deduce eternity.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi conclude that the Creator must be eternal?
A) Because the world is eternal
B) Because a non-eternal Creator would need an originator, causing infinite regress
C) Because the Creator is self-evident
D) Because the world is composed of accidents

Key Takeaway
The eternity of the Creator is a necessary conclusion from the impossibility of an infinite chain of causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the chain of causes cannot go back infinitely, then you must stand before the Eternal One. Have you truly acknowledged that your existence depends on a Being who has no beginning? What would change in your daily life if you lived with that awareness?
?
The proof shows that the Maker is eternal, without beginning. But does your worship reflect the eternity of the One you worship? Do you treat Him as if He is temporary or subject to change, while you go about your day?
?
If infinite regress is impossible, then there is a First Cause who is the origin of all things. Are you living as though that First Cause is real and present? What step can you take today to turn your theoretical belief into a living reality?

Proof of the Eternity of Takwin from the World's Origination
A proof for the eternity of takwin from the origination of the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that takwin (God's attribute of bringing things into existence) is eternal, not temporal.

Core Idea
The world's origination requires an eternal attribute of origination in God.

This passage presents a crisp theological argument that anchors the attribute of takwin in eternity. The author begins with the observable fact that the world is originated—it came into being after not existing—and then reasons backward to the eternal attribute that made that origination possible. This lesson walks through each step of that reasoning, showing why the attribute itself cannot be temporal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Get a feel for the flow of the argument without overanalyzing.
The Arabic uses technical terms; notice 'التكوين' and 'صفة الإحداث'.
2
Identify the key premises
The argument has four clear premises: (1) the world is originated, (2) God is its originator, (3) origination occurs through an attribute of origination, (4) the world cannot originate itself. Write them down in your own words.
3
Understand the logical chain
Trace how the premises lead to the conclusion that takwin is eternal. Ask: what would happen if takwin were temporal? The passage implies an infinite regress, which is impossible.
This is a classic kalam argument from contingency.
4
Reflect on the impossibility of self-origination
The passage explicitly states that the world cannot originate itself. Ponder why this is impossible—it would require the world to exist before it exists.
This is known as the argument against circular causation.
5
Confirm the conclusion
The conclusion is that takwin is an eternal attribute, not a temporal act. Make sure you see how the premises necessitate this.
The attribute by which God creates is as eternal as God Himself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يبرهن على ثبوت التكوين في الأزل بأن العالم محدث، ويثبت أن الله تعالى هو الذي أحدثه من العدم، وإحداثه يكون بصفة الإحداث، وهي التكوين، لأنه يستحيل أن يحدث العالم نفسه بنفسه، كما يستحيل أن يكون إيجاده بجزء منه، كما هو مبين في كتب علم الكلام، وخاصة المواقف.
Then he proves the establishment of takwin (التكوين) in pre-eternity by [the fact] that the world is originated (muḥdath), and he establishes that Allah Almighty is the One who originated it from non-existence, and its origination is by an attribute of origination (ṣifat al-iḥdāth), which is takwin, because it is impossible for the world to originate itself by itself, just as it is impossible for its coming into existence to be by a part of it, as is explained in the books of kalām, especially al-Mawāqif.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents a concise proof for the eternity of takwin. The argument proceeds as follows: (1) The world is originated (muḥdath) – it came into existence after not existing. (2) Its origination must be caused by God, who brought it from non-existence. (3) God's act of origination must occur through an attribute of origination (ṣifat al-iḥdāth), and that attribute is takwin. (4) The world cannot originate itself, because that would be circular, nor can it be brought into existence by a part of itself. Therefore, the attribute by which God brings things into existence must be eternal, since if it were temporal, it would need another attribute, leading to an infinite regress. The conclusion is that takwin is an eternal attribute.

Key Terms
التكوين
حدوث العالم
صفة الإحداث
محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that takwin is a temporal event like creation, happening at the moment the world began.
Correction: The proof shows that takwin is the eternal attribute by which temporal creation occurs; it is not itself created or temporal.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can't the world originate itself?
A) Because it is impossible for a thing to be its own originator
B) Because it does not have the power to originate
C) Because it is made of atoms that cannot act
D) Because it is eternal and does not need origination

Key Takeaway
Takwin is the eternal attribute by which God brings everything into existence; it is not a temporal act but the very basis of all temporal creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If takwin is an eternal attribute of God, meaning His act of creating is not a one-time event but a continuous attribute, do you truly see your existence at every moment as directly sustained by Him? How would that change the way you face difficulties or successes?
?
The world cannot originate itself—it requires an eternal creator. Have you ever acted as though you are the source of your own achievements or destiny? What would it mean to live with the constant awareness that your very existence is caused by God's eternal attribute of takwin?
?
Since God's attribute of origination is eternal, it means that before the world existed, God was already 'the Creator' in His essence. Does this challenge how you think about God's relationship with time? How does that affect your trust in His plans for your future?

Quranic Proofs of Temporality and the Creator
Nasafi enumerates Quranic proofs for temporality, Creator, and resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qurʾān's own content serves as a proof of the world's temporality, the impossibility of divinity for temporal beings, and the truth of resurrection.

Core Idea
The Qurʾān's arguments are a miracle because they reveal rational proofs, not just linguistic beauty.

The Qurʾān is not only a miracle in its language but also in the rational arguments it contains. Nasafi highlights several theological proofs embedded in the Qurʾān, from indications of the world's beginning to refutations of false beliefs. In this passage, he lists these proofs, showing that the Qurʾān itself is a comprehensive source of evidence for Islamic creed.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Let the list of proofs stand out.
The passage is a single sentence enumeration.
2
Identify the six proofs
Look for each distinct proof Nasafi mentions: temporality, impossibility of divinity for temporal beings, proofs of the Maker, disputation, refutation of natures, and resurrection.
Each proof is separated by 'واو' or 'و'.
3
Notice the example verse
Nasafi cites Q 13:4 as an example of refuting the theory of natures. Consider how a verse about agricultural patches can be a proof of design.
4
Reflect on why this is miraculous
Ask yourself why these rational arguments are considered part of the Quran's inimitability (iʿjāz). The commentary will help answer.
This connects to the core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن دلائل الإعجاز أيضا: ما تضمنه القرآن من إشارات تدل على حدوث العالم، وبيان استحالة القول بإلهية من تعتريه الحوادث، وتتعاقب الأضداد عليه ودلالات ثبوت الصانع، ومحاجة اليهود والنصارى والمشركين، وبطلان القول بالطبائع من نحو قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَفِي الْأَرْضِ قِطَعٌ مُتَجَاوِرَاتٌ ﴾ [الرعد: 4] ودلالة إثبات البعث، وهو من الأمور الغيبية.
And among the proofs of inimitability (iʿjāz) also: what the Qurʾān contains of indications that point to the temporal origination (ḥudūth) of the world, and the exposition of the impossibility of asserting divinity for one who is subject to accidents (ḥawādith) and upon whom opposites succeed one another, and proofs of the existence of the Maker (al-Ṣāniʿ), and the disputation with the Jews, Christians, and polytheists, and the invalidity of the doctrine of natures (ṭabāʾiʿ) – such as His statement, exalted is He: 'And in the earth are neighboring tracts' (Q 13:4) – and the indication of establishing resurrection (al-baʿth), which is among the unseen matters (al-umūr al-ghaybiyya).
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage enumerates the miraculous aspects of the Qurʾān that serve as proofs. The author lists several: (1) indications of the world's temporal creation, (2) demonstration that whatever undergoes accidents and changes cannot be divine, (3) proofs of the existence of the Creator, (4) arguments against Jews, Christians, and polytheists, (5) refutation of the theory of natural elements, citing Q 13:4 as an example, and (6) proof of resurrection, which is a matter of the unseen. Thus the Qurʾān's content itself is a miracle.

The Rational Proof from Intention and Outcome
Al-Nasafi presents a rational proof for negating creative power in humans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the gap between what a person intends and what actually happens proves that the true Creator is God alone.

Core Idea
Human actions contradict intentions, proving the true Creator is God.

This lesson examines Imam al-Nasafi’s rational argument that human beings do not possess the power to create their own actions. By observing that our intended outcomes often differ from actual results, reason points to God as the sole originator. The passage uses a simple but powerful logical deduction from everyday experience.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the clear logical flow from observation to conclusion.
The argument is concise — every word carries meaning.
2
Identify the key observation
The passage begins by stating that human actions do not come out as intended; they often contradict the intended description. Identify this observation and understand why it is the foundation of the proof.
Example: you intend to write neatly but end up with a mistake.
3
Follow the conditional reasoning
The author uses a conditional statement: if the servant had creative power, he would produce the action exactly as he wills. Since this does not happen, the real agent must be God. Trace this logic step by step.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that the true originator is other than the servant — God. Ask yourself: why is this proof considered rational? How does it fit with Islamic theology?
This proof is a standard kalam argument for divine causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعقل يشهد بأن العبد ليس له قدرة التخليق، وذلك لأن أفعال العباد لا تخرج على ما يقصده العبد من الوصف لفعله، بل تخرج على ما يضاد ذلك الوصف، ولو كان العبد له قدرة الاختراع لأوجد الفعل على ما يريد من الوصف، وحيث لم يوجد الفعل على وفق مراده دل أن الموجد لذلك غيره، وهو الله عز وجل. وأن إثبات قدرة التخليق لغير الله تعالى يؤدي إلى المحال، من ذلك المحال مثلا أنه يؤدي إلى القول بتعجيز الله، أو كونه ممنوعا. كما يؤدي إلى إبطال دلائل الموحدين على توحيد الصانع، وتعجيز الموحدين عن إثبات وحدانية الصانع ومنها أن وجود محدث لم يوجده الله تعالى يؤدي إلى كون العالم بصانعين، ومن ذلك وقوع التشابه بين فعل القديم والمحدث، وكذلك يؤدي إلى عبادة غير الله.
And reason bears witness that the servant does not have the power of creation (qudrat al-takhlīq), and that is because the actions of servants do not come out according to the description that the servant intends for his action, but rather they come out contrary to that description. If the servant had the power of invention (qudrat al-ikhtirāʿ), he would bring about the action according to the description he wills. And since the action does not occur in accordance with his desire, this indicates that the one who brings it into existence is other than him, and He is God Almighty. And affirming the power of creation for other than God Almighty leads to impossibility (al-muḥāl). Among that impossibility is that it leads to the assertion of disabling God (taʿjīz Allāh) or that He is prevented. It also leads to nullifying the proofs of the monotheists (al-muwaḥḥidūn) for the oneness of the Maker, and disabling the monotheists from proving the unity of the Maker. And among them is that the existence of an originated thing not brought into existence by God Almighty leads to the world having two makers, and from that is the occurrence of similarity between the action of the Eternal and the originated. And it also leads to the worship of other than God.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof for the negation of creative power in human beings. It begins with the observation that a person's actions often do not match their intended description; for example, one may intend to write beautifully but produce an error. If the servant had the power to create, he would actualize his intention precisely. Since he does not, the true agent must be God. The passage then warns that attributing creation to anyone besides God leads to absurdities: it implies God can be disabled or prevented, undermines the monotheistic proofs for the oneness of the Creator, suggests the possibility of multiple creators, conflates the eternal and the temporal, and ultimately leads to polytheism. The proof is direct: discrepancy between will and outcome disproves creative power in humans.

Key Terms
قدرة التخليق
قدرة الاختراع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If humans do not create their actions, they have no action at all.
Correction: The passage only negates creative power (ikhtirāʿ), not all action. Humans still acquire actions through their choices, but the actual bringing into existence is from God.

Checkpoint
What evidence does al-Nasafi use to show that the servant lacks creative power?
A) Humans often fail to achieve their goals
B) Human actions frequently contradict the intended description
C) Humans cannot create matter from nothing
D) The Qur'an explicitly denies creative power to humans

Key Takeaway
The discrepancy between intention and outcome in human actions rationally proves that the true originator of actions is God alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you experienced a clear gap between your intention and the actual outcome of an action? Did you humble yourself and realize your dependence on God, or did you blame yourself or others? After today's lesson, will you see such moments as reminders that you are not the ultimate creator?
?
If you truly believe that God alone creates the outcome of your actions, how will this change the way you plan your day? Will you still rely on your own power, or will you start your tasks by acknowledging that only God can bring about what you intend?
?
Consider an action you perform repeatedly, like praying or studying. In those actions, do you often feel in control of the outcome? How does the truth that God brings about even the quality of your prayer (whether it is attentive or distracted) affect your view of your own efforts and your need for His mercy?

The Impossibilities of Attributing Creation to Others
Al-Nasafi enumerates the absurdities of attributing creative power to anyone besides God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological absurdities that arise if one claims that any being other than God has creative power.

Core Idea
Attributing creation to others leads to disabling God, polytheism, and destroying tawheed.

After establishing that humans lack creative power, Imam al-Nasafi warns against the dangerous consequences of claiming otherwise. This lesson explores the cascade of impossibilities that follow: from implying God can be prevented, to undermining the proofs of monotheism, to suggesting multiple creators and ultimately polytheism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on consequences
Read the Arabic and translation for the second part of the source. Notice that it lists several impossibilities (muḥālāt) in a cumulative manner.
The word 'muḥāl' is key — it means rationally impossible.
2
Identify each impossibility
The passage mentions four main consequences: (1) disabling God, (2) nullifying monotheistic proofs, (3) implying two creators, (4) equating Creator and creature. Identify each.
These are not just theological but also rational contradictions.
3
Understand why each is impossible
For each consequence, ask: why is it considered rationally impossible in Islamic theology? Reflect on the attributes of God (omnipotence, uniqueness) and how these are violated.
For example, ta'jiz Allah contradicts His attribute of absolute power.
4
Connect back to tawheed
The core issue is that creative power belongs solely to Allah. Any deviation compromises the foundation of tawheed. Think about how this passage strengthens the monotheistic stance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And reason bears witness that the servant does not have the power of creation (qudrat al-takhlīq), and that is because the actions of servants do not come out according to the description that the servant intends for his action, but rather they come out contrary to that description. All God's Actions are Good and Two Types of Shirk
Al-Nasafi concludes that all divine actions are good and identifies two types of shirk.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position that all divine actions are good and wise, and the distinction between shirk in creation and shirk in worship.

Core Idea
All God's acts are good; shirk wrongly partners others in creation or worship.

This passage from al-Nasafi encapsulates a key Maturidi conclusion: after proving that God is the sole creator, it follows that all His actions are good and wise. It then clarifies the two forms of shirk that contradict tawhid, showing how creatures are not partners to God in any sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how al-Nasafi moves from affirming divine goodness to defining shirk.
The text ends abruptly; the full argument is completed in the commentary.
2
Understand the claim about divine actions
Al-Nasafi asserts that everything from God is good (ḥasan) and wise (ḥikma). This is a direct repudiation of dualist views that ascribe evil to God.
This aligns with the Maturidi emphasis on divine wisdom.
3
Identify the two types of shirk
Find the two categories: shirk in creation (like the Magians) and shirk in worship (like idolaters). Notice the different objects of partnership.
The first denies God's sole creativity; the second denies His sole worthiness of worship.
4
Connect to tawhid
Reflect on how the passage reinforces tawhid: if God is the sole creator and independent, then nothing shares in His attributes or rights.
This is the logical conclusion of the preceding proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتحدث بإسهاب عن الحسن و القبح مثبتا أن كل ما صدر عن الله سبحانه فهو حسن وهو حكمة، وينطلق بعد ذلك ليقول : إن أفعال الخلق كلها مخلوقة الله، وهذا يؤدي إلى أن العباد ليسوا شركاء لربهم، فإن الشرك نوعان: شرك يثبت فيه المشرك الله شريكا في خلق العالم مثل المجوس وشرك يثبت فيه المشرك شريكا الله تعالى في استحقاق العبادة مثل عبدة الأصنام. وإذا كان الله هو خالق كل شيء، وليس في احتياج إلى أحد، كان وحده
And he discusses at length about good (al-ḥasan) and evil (al-qabīḥ), affirming that everything that proceeds from God, glorified is He, is good and is wisdom. Then he proceeds to say: Indeed, all the actions of creatures are created by God, and this leads to the fact that servants are not partners to their Lord. For shirk is of two types: shirk in which the polytheist affirms a partner to God in the creation of the world, like the Magians (al-majūs); and shirk in which the polytheist affirms a partner to God in deserving worship, like idol worshippers. And if God is the Creator of everything, and is not in need of anyone, He alone...
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion on good and evil by reaffirming that all God's actions are good and wise. It then draws the consequence of God being the sole Creator: creatures cannot be partners to God. Two types of shirk are distinguished: (1) shirk in creation (rubūbiyya), attributing a share in creating the universe (as the Magians posit a creator of evil); and (2) shirk in worship (ulūhiyya), ascribing divinity to others. The passage ends with the statement that since God is the Creator of all and independent, He alone deserves worship. This reinforces tawhid and the conclusion of the earlier proofs.

القبح

عبدة الأصنام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think shirk only means worshipping idols, but the passage shows it can also mean affirming a partner in creation.
Correction: Shirk includes both ascribing creative power to others (like the Magians) and directing worship to others (like idol worshippers). Both violate tawhid.

Checkpoint

A) They worship idols
B) They affirm a partner to God in creation
C) They deny the existence of God
D) They reject prophets

Key Takeaway
All divine actions are good and wise; shirk either partners others in creation or in worship, both contradicting tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Have you ever subtly directed your worship—like hope, fear, or love—towards someone or something other than Allah? The second type of shirk is hidden. Examine your heart: Is there any attachment that competes with your devotion? What will you do to purify it starting today?

intermediate
6 min
God's Will Prevails Over Creature's Will
Nasafi argues against creature's will overpowering God's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is impossible for a creature's will to overrule God's will, and how this argument refutes dualism and the proof of mutual prevention.

Core Idea
God's will cannot be thwarted by any creature's will.

This lesson examines a powerful argument from Imam al-Nasafi (a Maturidi theologian) demonstrating that God's will must be supreme over any creature's will. Nasafi uses a hypothetical scenario to show that if a creature's will could prevail over God's, it would imply weakness in God—a clear absurdity. The argument also addresses broader theological implications, refuting both the polytheist proof of mutual prevention and dualism.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Notice the logical structure: a conditional statement followed by its consequences.
Pay attention to the key terms: مشيئة (will) and أنفذ (more effective).
2
Understand the hypothetical scenario
Nasafi sets up a scenario: God wills faith from a disbeliever, the disbeliever wills disbelief, and disbelief happens. Identify why this scenario is problematic.
The conclusion is that God's will would be thwarted and the creature's will would be more effective.
3
Grasp the reductio ad absurdum
Nasafi explains that such a situation would imply God's weakness and incapacity, like a king whose orders are not carried out. Describe such a thing is impossible for God.
This is a classic argument from impossibility.
4
Connect to broader debates
See how this argument refutes the proof of mutual prevention (دلالة التمانع) and would actually support dualism. Understand why this is a strong defense of divine omnipotence.
Dualism (مذهب الثنوية) posits two competing divine wills.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقول : أنه تعالى لو شاء من الكافر الإيمان والكافر شاء من نفسه الكفر ثم كان الكفر دون الإيمان، لتعطلت مشيئة الله، وكانت مشيئة الكافر أنفذ من مشيئة الله، ومن أول الدلائل على عجز الملك وضعفه أن يقع في ملكه ما لا يشاء ويشاء أشياء فلا تقع، ووصف الله بذلك محال. وهذا الكلام يرد على دلالة التمانع بالإبطال ويؤدي إلى جعل مذهب الثنوية صحيحا.
Then he says: If God willed from the disbeliever faith, and the disbeliever willed from himself disbelief, and then disbelief occurred instead of faith, then God's will would be thwarted, and the disbeliever's will would be more effective than God's will. And from the first proofs of the king's incapacity and weakness is that something occurs in his kingdom that he does not will, and he wills things that do not occur. And describing God with that is impossible. And this statement refutes the indication of mutual prevention (دلالة التمانع) and leads to making the dualist doctrine correct.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a proof by reductio ad absurdum. Nasafi presents a hypothetical: if God wills faith from a disbeliever, and the disbeliever wills disbelief, and then disbelief occurs, then God's will would be rendered ineffective and the creature's will would be more powerful. This is logically absurd because it would imply God's incapacity and weakness, akin to a king whose will is not executed in his kingdom. Such a description of God is impossible. Furthermore, this scenario would undermine the proof of mutual prevention (دلالة التمانع) which asserts that if multiple gods existed, disorder would occur. In fact, this scenario would actually support dualism (مذهب الثنوية) because it suggests a conflict of wills. Therefore, it is impossible for a creature's will to oppose God's will effectively.

Key Terms
مشيئة الله
أنفذ
دلالة التمانع
مذهب الثنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this argument proves that God predestines all actions, eliminating human free will (fatalism/determinism).
Correction: The argument does not deny human will; it only establishes that God's will is supreme and cannot be overridden. Human will exists but is subordinate. The passage does not address whether humans have real choice, only that if a creature's will conflicted with God's, God's will must prevail.

Checkpoint
What logical consequence does Nasafi draw if a disbeliever's will were to overcome God's will?
A) It would prove God's mercy
B) It would imply God's incapacity and support dualism
C) It would show that humans have absolute free will
D) It would have no theological implications

Key Takeaway
God's will is absolutely effective and cannot be thwarted by any creature's will; otherwise, God would be weak—a clear impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your own will is always subordinate to God's? Consider a specific area in your life where you have insisted on something that contradicts what God has commanded. In light of this lesson, what does that reveal about your conviction of God's absolute sovereignty? Are you willing to surrender that area completely to His will?
?
If God's will is absolutely effective, then what does it say about your daily worries and fears? Do you live as if God's will can be thwarted by your plans or by the actions of others? Examine your heart: are there areas where you secretly doubt that God's will will prevail? How would your life change if you truly internalized that nothing happens without His will?
?
The passage uses the analogy of a king whose commands are not executed as a sign of weakness. In your life, are there areas where you act as if God's commands are optional or can be ignored without consequence? What steps will you take this week to align your actions with the certainty that God's will is always realized?

Substance vs. Accident and the Refutation of the Mu'tazila
The author defines substance and accident and refutes the Mu'tazila who allowed accidents without a subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational distinction between substance (al-'ayn) and accident (al-'arad), and why accidents cannot exist without a subject.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot exist without a subject; this is known by rational necessity.

Having clarified the terminology, the author now applies it to distinguish substance from accident. He defines each in terms of the necessity of a subject and then refutes those who denied this basic rational truth. This lesson will walk you through the definitions and the refutation, grounding a foundational principle of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own progression from definition to refutation become clear.
The passage is concise but packed with argument.
2
Identify the definitions of substance and accident
Locate where the author defines 'substance' (al-'ayn) as that whose existence is possible not in a subject, and 'accident' (al-'arad) as that whose existence is impossible not in a subject. Notice the phrase 'by the necessity of the intellect'.
This is a rational, not empirical, distinction.
3
Understand the refutation of the Mu'tazila
The author claims that the Mu'tazila and Ibn al-Rawandi were forced to permit accidents without a subject due to their corrupt principles. He asserts that whatever leads to the impossible is itself impossible.
This refutation relies on the rational necessity established earlier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرف ما هو المراد بهذه اللفظة ظهرت صحته وعلم به امتیاز العرض من العين إذ العين ما يصح وجوده لا في محل، والعرض يستحيل وجوده لا في محل، إذ يعرف ببديهة العقل استحالة وجود حركة لا بمتحرك غير قائمة به وكذا في السواد والبياض وكل عرض. وكثير من المعتزلة وابن الراوندي اضطروا إلى ركوب هذا المحال وتجويز عرض حادث لا في محل، أداهم إليه أصولهم الفاسدة، وما أدى إلى المحال محال نبين ذلك عند انتهائنا إلى تلك المسائل إن شاء الله تعالى.
And when it is known what is meant by this term, its correctness becomes apparent, and by it the distinction of the accident (al-'arad) from the substance (al-'ayn) is known: for the substance (al-'ayn) is that whose existence is possible not in a subject (mahall), and the accident (al-'arad) is that whose existence is impossible not in a subject, for it is known by the necessity of the intellect (badīhat al-'aql) the impossibility of the existence of a motion without a moving thing not subsisting in it, and likewise in blackness and whiteness and every accident. And many of the Mu'tazila and Ibn al-Rawandi were forced to mount this impossible thing and to permit an originated accident not in a subject; their corrupt principles led them to that, and that which leads to the impossible is impossible. We will clarify that when we reach those questions, if God wills.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation. It begins by stating that once the intended meaning of the term 'accident' is understood, the distinction between substance and accident becomes clear. It defines substance as that which can exist not in a subject (i.e., it is self-subsistent), and accident as that which cannot exist not in a subject (i.e., it needs a subject). This is known by the necessity of reason: we intuitively know that motion cannot exist without a moving thing, nor blackness without a black thing, etc. The author then refutes the Mu'tazila and Ibn al-Rawandi, who claimed that an accident could exist without a subject, calling this an impossible position ('muhāl') to which their corrupt principles led them. He asserts that whatever leads to the impossible is itself impossible, and promises to discuss this further later.

Key Terms
العين (al-'ayn)
العرض (al-'arad)
المحل (al-mahall)
بديهة العقل (badīhat al-'aql)
المحال (al-muhāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think accidents can exist without a subject.
Correction: The author shows this is impossible by rational necessity; accidents by definition require a subject.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is the impossibility of an accident existing without a subject known?
A) By revelation and scripture
B) By the necessity of the intellect (badīhat al-'aql)
C) By majority consensus
D) By empirical observation

Key Takeaway
The rational necessity that accidents require a subject is a foundational principle in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief that felt convenient but contradicted what your intellect clearly knows to be true? How does the Mu'tazila's error warn you about prioritizing man-made principles over clear rational necessity?
?
The author says it is impossible for an accident to exist without a subject. In your life, do you treat certain 'accidents'—like your mood, desires, or thoughts—as if they exist independently from your soul? How might this lesson correct that and push you to take responsibility for your inner states?
?
Rational necessity (badīhat al-'aql) is a foundational tool in Islamic theology. Have you allowed doubts, cultural influences, or philosophical trends to cloud your trust in this God-given clarity? What concrete step will you take today to rely more on clear intellectual principles in your faith, even when they are uncomfortable?
