The Refutation of Infinite Divisibility of the Atom
Nasafi argues against infinite divisibility and answers an objection on infinity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why infinite divisibility of matter is absurd and how the objection from God's infinite attributes is refuted by Nasafi.

Core Idea
Infinite parts make the mountain equal to a mustard seed.

Imam Nasafi presents a key argument in Islamic theology against the idea that physical bodies can be divided infinitely. He shows that such a view contradicts observable reality, using the example of a mustard seed and a mountain. He then anticipates and refutes an objection based on God's infinite knowledge and power, demonstrating that the impossible cannot be used to justify another impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Note the names and the key claim about infinite divisibility.
2
Understand the reductio argument
Identify how the claim leads to the absurd conclusion that a mountain is not larger than a mustard seed. Notice the role of 'mushahada' (observed fact).
3
Examine the objection
See how the opponents use God's infinite knowledge and power to argue that one infinite can be greater than another. Understand the analogy.
4
Follow Nasafi's response
Nasafi rejects the analogy as invalid because it is based on an impossibility (muhal). He also notes that the objects of God's knowledge and power are limited.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why defending the indivisible atom (jawhar) was important for the Kalam worldview, and how this argument preserves the coherence of physics and theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم في هذه الأقسام التي بيناها اختلاف بين المتكلمين فأنكر هشام بن الحكم، والنظام وكثير من الأوائل والحساب، وجود ما سميناه جوهرا وهو الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ، وزعموا أن الجزء وإن قل فهو يتجزأ إلى ما لا نهاية له وهذا يؤدي إلى أن الخردلة لا تكون أصغر من الجبل، ولا يكون الجبل أكبر منها إذ ما لا نهاية لأجزائه لا يكون أكبر مما لا نهاية لأجزائه، إذ الكبر في الأجسام يراد به كثرة الأجزاء وزيادة أجزاء أحدهما على الآخر، وما لا نهاية له لا يكون أزيد مما لا نهاية له. والقول بأن الجبل ليس بأعظم من الخردلة إنكار المشاهدة. فإن قالوا إن معلومات الله تعالى أكثر من مقدوراته لما أن ذاته معلوم له وليس مقدور له، وإن كان لا نهاية لمعلوماته ومقدوراته فيثبت بهذا جواز كون ما لا نهاية له أكثر مما لا نهاية له. قلنا: هذا محال لما بينا والمحال لا يصحح بالنظر. ثم قيل له: ما من معلومات الله تعالى ومقدوراته محصورة ومعلوماته أكثر، فأما ما لم يوجد
Then concerning these divisions we have mentioned, there is disagreement among the mutakallimun. Hisham ibn al-Hakam, al-Nazzam, and many of the ancients and calculators denied the existence of what we have called the atom (jawhar), which is the part that is not further divisible. And they claimed that the part, however small, is divisible into infinity. And this leads to the mustard seed not being smaller than the mountain, nor the mountain larger than it, because that which has infinite parts cannot be larger than that which has infinite parts, since magnitude in bodies is understood as multiplicity of parts and increase of parts of one over the other, and what is infinite cannot be greater than what is infinite. And the statement that the mountain is not greater than the mustard seed is a denial of what is witnessed. And if they say: The knowledge of Allah Most High is more than His power, since His essence is known to Him but not within His power, and if His knowledge and power are infinite, then it is established by this that it is possible for one infinite to be more than another infinite. We say: This is impossible (muhal) for what we have shown, and the impossible cannot be validated by analogical reasoning (nazar). Then it was said to him: The things that are known to Allah and the things that are within His power are limited (mahsura) and His knowledge is more. As for that which does not exist...
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the doctrine of infinite divisibility of the atom (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazza'). The author first notes that Hisham ibn al-Hakam, al-Nazzam, and many ancient philosophers and mathematicians denied the existence of the indivisible part (i.e., the atom). They claimed that any part, no matter how small, is divisible ad infinitum. The author then draws a reductio ad absurdum: if parts were infinite, then a mustard seed and a mountain would have an equal number of parts, so the mountain would not be larger than the seed. But observation (mushahada) contradicts this. Next, the author anticipates an objection: the objectors might point to God's knowledge and power—both infinite, yet God's knowledge is more than His power (since His essence is known but not within His power). This would allow for one infinite to be greater than another. The author responds that this analogy is impossible (muhal) because the impossibility has already been established, and the impossible cannot be used to validate a position through reasoning. Then the author adds a further response: actually, the things known and the things within God's power are limited (mahsura), and His knowledge is more. However, the passage cuts off at 'as for that which does not exist...' leaving the argument incomplete.

Key Terms
جزء لا يتجزأ (juz' la yatajazza')
جوهر (jawhar)
لا نهاية (la nihaya)
خردلة (khurdala)
مشاهدة (mushahada)
محال (muhal)
مقدورات (maqdurat)
معلومات (ma'lumat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument against infinite divisibility rejects all forms of infinity, including mathematical or divine infinity.
Correction: The argument only rejects infinite divisibility of physical bodies, not all infinities. The objection from God's attributes is addressed separately.

Checkpoint
What is the main consequence of infinite divisibility according to the passage?
A) The mountain would be larger than the mustard seed
B) The mountain and the mustard seed would be equal in size
C) The number of parts would be finite
D) The atom would be impossible

Key Takeaway
Infinite divisibility of matter is refuted because it leads to an absurd contradiction with observed reality, and objections based on divine infinity are invalid because they rely on an impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever clung to a logically coherent belief that contradicted what you could plainly see? This passage shows that when reasoning leads to absurdity, observation must win. What 'mountain-and-mustard-seed' contradiction are you living with right now, and will you finally surrender it to reality?
?
The opponents used God's infinity to justify an impossibility. Are there theological concepts or God's attributes that you have twisted to defend your own flawed reasoning? Name one such case and commit to re-examining it with the same rigor Nasafi applies.
?
Nasafi says 'the impossible cannot be validated by analogical reasoning.' In what area of your life have you built a case for something using analogies that have no real connection? Will you dismantle that argument today, even if it costs you a comfortable belief?

Three Views on the World's Origin
Classifies three groups on the world's creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three broad positions on the world's origin as categorized by Maturidi theologians.

Core Idea
The world's origin is debated among three groups based on substance and manufacture.

Before engaging with the detailed refutations, we must first map the intellectual landscape. The author presents a threefold classification of opinions on the world's creation, which sets the stage for the ensuing theological argument. This classification is essential for understanding why the eternalist position is the primary target.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the classification of views.
2
Identify the three groups
Note each group's position on substance (al-ṭīna) and manufacture (al-ṣanʿa).
The groups are: fully originated, fully eternal, and mixed.
3
Understand the people of truth
Recognize which group is identified as the people of truth (ahl al-ḥaqq) and why.
They hold that both substance and manufacture are originated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرف انحصار جميع أجزاء العالم على ما بينا من الأقسام، وعرف القديم والمحدث فنقول: افترق الناس في أمر العالم على أقسام ثلاثة: قال بعضهم وهم أهل الحق: إنه بجميع أقسامه وأجزائه محدث كائن بعد أن لم يكن، فكان حديث الطينة والصنعة. وقال بعض الناس: هو قديم الطينة والصنعة لا ابتداء لشيء منه البتة عرضا كان أو جوهرا بل هو بجميع أقسامه لم يزل ولا يزال، وهؤلاء يسمون لم يزلية. وقال بعضهم: العالم قديم الطينة حديث الصنعة. ثم افترق القائلون بقدم الطينة والصنعة جميعا فقالت طائفة منهم: هو قديم لم يزل على ما نشاهده، ولا يزال أبدا كذلك ولا محدث له ولا مكون لاستغناء القديم عما يتعلق وجوده به، إذ هو المستغني بوجوده عن غيره، وشبهتهم أنهم قالوا: لو كان العالم محدثا لصار موجودا بإيجاد غيره إياه، ولو كان كذلك لكان الإيجاد إما ذات الموجد القديم، وإما ذات الموجد المحدث، وإما معنى هو غير هما، ولا جائز أن يكون ذات الموجد القديم لأنه يقتضي وجوده في الأزل لوجود ما هو إيجاده، ووجود الحادث في الأزل محال. وكذا تعلق وجود ما هو موجود في الأزل بالإيجاد محال، وإن كان الإيجاد ذات الموجد والإيجاد هو الموجد والإحداث هو المحدث فإذا لا تعلق لوجود العالم بغيره فكان قديما. وإن كان الإيجاد معنى وراء الموجد والعالم فهو إما أن يكون قائما بنفسه، وإما أن يكون قائما بغيره ومحال قيامه بنفسه لكونه صفة في نفسه وقيامه بغيره متنوع إلى قيامه بالموجد أو الموجد وقيامه بالموجد الحادث محال لما فيه من إخراج القديم من أن يكون موجدا إياه، قيامه بالموجد متنوع إلى كون الإيجاد قديما وكونه حديثا، فإن كان حديثا فالقديم ليس بمحل الحوادث، وإن كان قديما فقدمه يقتضي قدم الموجد الحادث، والقديم يستحيل تعلق وجوده بغيره، وإذا كان كونه محدثا ينقسم إلى هذه الأقسام وهي باطلة دل أن القول بحدوث العالم أو حدوث شيء من أجزائه باطل
And when the encompassing of all parts of the world according to the divisions we have explained is known, and the eternal (al-qadīm) and the originated (al-muḥdath) are known, we say: People have divided concerning the affair of the world into three divisions: Some of them—and they are the people of truth—said: It is, in all its divisions and parts, originated (muḥdath), coming into existence after it was not, so it was originated in substance (al-ṭīna) and manufacture (al-ṣanʿa). And some people said: It is eternal in substance and manufacture, with no beginning for any part of it at all, whether accident or substance, rather it is in all its divisions never ceasing and never ending, and these are called the 'Lam Yuzaliyya' (the Never Ceasing). And some of them said: The world is eternal in substance, originated in manufacture. Then those who said it is eternal in both substance and manufacture split, and a group among them said: It is eternal, never ceasing as we observe it, and will never cease to be so forever, and there is no originator (muḥdith) for it nor maker (mukawwin), because the eternal is independent of that which its existence depends on, since it is independent by its existence of other than itself. And their sophism (shubha) is that they said: If the world were originated, it would come to exist through the bringing into existence (ījād) of another than itself. And if that were so, then that bringing into existence would be either the essence (dhāt) of the eternal bringer into existence (al-mūjid al-qadīm), or the essence of an originated bringer into existence (al-mūjid al-muḥdath), or a meaning (maʿnā) other than both. And it is not possible that it be the essence of the eternal bringer into existence, because that would necessitate its existence in pre-eternity (al-azal) due to the existence of that which is its bringing into existence, and the existence of the originated in pre-eternity is impossible. Likewise, the attachment of the existence of that which exists in pre-eternity to the bringing into existence is impossible. And if the bringing into existence were the essence of the bringer into existence, and the bringing into existence is the bringer into existence and the originating (al-iḥdāth) is the originator (al-muḥdith), then there is no attachment of the world's existence to other than it, so it would be eternal. And if the bringing into existence were a meaning separate from the bringer into existence and the world, then it must either subsist by itself or subsist by other than itself. Its subsistence by itself is impossible because it is an attribute in itself. And its subsistence by other than itself varies between its subsistence in the bringer into existence or the brought into existence (al-mūjad). And its subsistence in the originated bringer into existence is impossible because it would remove the eternal from being a bringer into existence for it. Its subsistence in the bringer into existence varies between the bringing into existence being eternal or originated. If it is originated, then the eternal is not a locus for originated accidents. And if it is eternal, then its eternity necessitates the eternity of the originated brought into existence. And it is impossible for the existence of the eternal to be attached to other than it. And when its being originated is divided into these divisions, and they are false, it indicates that the statement that the world is originated or that any part of it is originated is false.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage begins by stating that after understanding the divisions of the world and the definitions of qadīm and muḥdath, we can classify people's opinions on the world into three groups. The first group (the people of truth) holds that the world is entirely originated in both substance and manufacture—it came to be after not being. The second group (called al-Lam Yuzaliyya) asserts that the world is eternal in both substance and manufacture, with no beginning whatsoever. The third group holds that the world is eternal in substance but originated in manufacture. The passage then focuses on those who affirm eternal in both substance and manufacture. A subgroup of them says the world is eternal as we observe it, with no creator, because the eternal is independent. Their sophism (shubha) is then presented: if the world were originated, it would require a cause (ījād) from another. That cause must be either the essence of an eternal agent, or the essence of an originated agent, or a distinct meaning. Each possibility is examined and shown to be impossible, leading to the conclusion that the world cannot be originated from their perspective. The refutation argues that if the cause were the essence of the eternal, then the effect would also be eternal, which is absurd. If the cause were a distinct meaning, then it would have to subsist, which is impossible for an attribute of an eternal being, and so on. Thus the argument of the eternalists is refuted, and the passage concludes that their claim that the world cannot be originated is false. Note: This refutation is presented within the context of the eternalists' own assumptions.

Key Terms
محدث (muḥdath)
قديم (qadīm)
الطينة (al-ṭīna)
الصنعة (al-ṣanʿa)
لم يزلية (Lam Yuzaliyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which group holds that the world is eternal in both substance and manufacture?
A) People of truth
B) Lam Yuzaliyya
C) Mixed view

Key Takeaway
The world's origin is categorized by substance and manufacture, with the truth being that both are originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that the world was created by Allah from nothing (both substance and manufacture originated), does your daily life reflect that contingency? In what areas do you take the world for granted as if it were self-sufficient?
?
The third group accepts a creator but believes matter is eternal. Do you ever act as if the material world is independent of Allah's constant creation, for example by attributing success solely to your own efforts without recognizing that even the atoms of your body are sustained by Him?
?
The Lam Yuzaliyya deny any creator entirely. In what subtle ways might you be living as if the world is self-created—perhaps by fearing natural processes more than the One who controls them, or by forgetting to thank Allah for the very existence of the things you use?

Refutation of the Absolute Eternalists
Refutes the Lam Yuzaliyya's argument for the world's eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument used by the absolute eternalists and how it is refuted through a systematic analysis of possible causes.

Core Idea
The eternalists' claim that the world cannot be created is refuted by showing that their own logic leads to contradictions.

The most radical group among those who affirm the world's eternity in both substance and manufacture is the Lam Yuzaliyya. They argue that if the world were originated, it would require a cause, which leads to impossible consequences. The author takes their argument seriously and dissects it, revealing its internal flaws. This lesson walks through their sophism and the detailed refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the sophism
Read the eternalists' argument that if the world were originated, it would need a cause, and that cause cannot be anything possible.
2
Analyze the three possibilities
The cause (ījād) could be the essence of an eternal agent, an originated agent, or a separate meaning. See why each is impossible.
3
Understand the refutation
Follow how each possibility leads to a contradiction, thus proving the original premise (that the world is not originated) is false.
4
Conclude
The passage concludes that the statement that the world is originated is false from the eternalists' perspective, but this shows their argument is unsound.
The world is actually originated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرف انحصار جميع أجزاء العالم على ما بينا من الأقسام، وعرف القديم والمحدث فنقول: افترق الناس في أمر العالم على أقسام ثلاثة: قال بعضهم وهم أهل الحق: إنه بجميع أقسامه وأجزائه محدث كائن بعد أن لم يكن، فكان حديث الطينة والصنعة. وقال بعض الناس: هو قديم الطينة والصنعة لا ابتداء لشيء منه البتة عرضا كان أو جوهرا بل هو بجميع أقسامه لم يزل ولا يزال، وهؤلاء يسمون لم يزلية. وقال بعضهم: العالم قديم الطينة حديث الصنعة. ثم افترق القائلون بقدم الطينة والصنعة جميعا فقالت طائفة منهم: هو قديم لم يزل على ما نشاهده، ولا يزال أبدا كذلك ولا محدث له ولا مكون لاستغناء القديم عما يتعلق وجوده به، إذ هو المستغني بوجوده عن غيره، وشبهتهم أنهم قالوا: لو كان العالم محدثا لصار موجودا بإيجاد غيره إياه، ولو كان كذلك لكان الإيجاد إما ذات الموجد القديم، وإما ذات الموجد المحدث، وإما معنى هو غير هما، ولا جائز أن يكون ذات الموجد القديم لأنه يقتضي وجوده في الأزل لوجود ما هو إيجاده، ووجود الحادث في الأزل محال. وكذا تعلق وجود ما هو موجود في الأزل بالإيجاد محال، وإن كان الإيجاد ذات الموجد والإيجاد هو الموجد والإحداث هو المحدث فإذا لا تعلق لوجود العالم بغيره فكان قديما. وإن كان الإيجاد معنى وراء الموجد والعالم فهو إما أن يكون قائما بنفسه، وإما أن يكون قائما بغيره ومحال قيامه بنفسه لكونه صفة في نفسه وقيامه بغيره متنوع إلى قيامه بالموجد أو الموجد وقيامه بالموجد الحادث محال لما فيه من إخراج القديم من أن يكون موجدا إياه، قيامه بالموجد متنوع إلى كون الإيجاد قديما وكونه حديثا، فإن كان حديثا فالقديم ليس بمحل الحوادث، وإن كان قديما فقدمه يقتضي قدم الموجد الحادث، والقديم يستحيل تعلق وجوده بغيره، وإذا كان كونه محدثا ينقسم إلى هذه الأقسام وهي باطلة دل أن القول بحدوث العالم أو حدوث شيء من أجزائه باطل
And when the encompassing of all parts of the world according to the divisions we have explained is known, and the eternal (al-qadīm) and the originated (al-muḥdath) are known, we say: People have divided concerning the affair of the world into three divisions: Some of them—and they are the people of truth—said: It is, in all its divisions and parts, originated (muḥdath), coming into existence after it was not, so it was originated in substance (al-ṭīna) and manufacture (al-ṣanʿa). And some people said: It is eternal in substance and manufacture, with no beginning for any part of it at all, whether accident or substance, rather it is in all its divisions never ceasing and never ending, and these are called the 'Lam Yuzaliyya' (the Never Ceasing). And some of them said: The world is eternal in substance, originated in manufacture. Then those who said it is eternal in both substance and manufacture split, and a group among them said: It is eternal, never ceasing as we observe it, and will never cease to be so forever, and there is no originator (muḥdith) for it nor maker (mukawwin), because the eternal is independent of that which its existence depends on, since it is independent by its existence of other than itself. And their sophism (shubha) is that they said: If the world were originated, it would come to exist through the bringing into existence (ījād) of another than itself. And if that were so, then that bringing into existence would be either the essence (dhāt) of the eternal bringer into existence (al-mūjid al-qadīm), or the essence of an originated bringer into existence (al-mūjid al-muḥdath), or a meaning (maʿnā) other than both. And it is not possible that it be the essence of the eternal bringer into existence, because that would necessitate its existence in pre-eternity (al-azal) due to the existence of that which is its bringing into existence, and the existence of the originated in pre-eternity is impossible. Likewise, the attachment of the existence of that which exists in pre-eternity to the bringing into existence is impossible. And if the bringing into existence were the essence of the bringer into existence, and the bringing into existence is the bringer into existence and the originating (al-iḥdāth) is the originator (al-muḥdith), then there is no attachment of the world's existence to other than it, so it would be eternal. And if the bringing into existence were a meaning separate from the bringer into existence and the world, then it must either subsist by itself or subsist by other than itself. Its subsistence by itself is impossible because it is an attribute in itself. And its subsistence by other than itself varies between its subsistence in the bringer into existence or the brought into existence (al-mūjad). And its subsistence in the originated bringer into existence is impossible because it would remove the eternal from being a bringer into existence for it. Its subsistence in the bringer into existence varies between the bringing into existence being eternal or originated. If it is originated, then the eternal is not a locus for originated accidents. And if it is eternal, then its eternity necessitates the eternity of the originated brought into existence. And it is impossible for the existence of the eternal to be attached to other than it. And when its being originated is divided into these divisions, and they are false, it indicates that the statement that the world is originated or that any part of it is originated is false.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage begins by stating that after understanding the divisions of the world and the definitions of qadīm and muḥdath, we can classify people's opinions on the world into three groups. The first group (the people of truth) holds that the world is entirely originated in both substance and manufacture—it came to be after not being. The second group (called al-Lam Yuzaliyya) asserts that the world is eternal in both substance and manufacture, with no beginning whatsoever. The third group holds that the world is eternal in substance but originated in manufacture. The passage then focuses on those who affirm eternal in both substance and manufacture. A subgroup of them says the world is eternal as we observe it, with no creator, because the eternal is independent. Their sophism (shubha) is then presented: if the world were originated, it would require a cause (ījād) from another. That cause must be either the essence of an eternal agent, or the essence of an originated agent, or a distinct meaning. Each possibility is examined and shown to be impossible, leading to the conclusion that the world cannot be originated from their perspective. The refutation argues that if the cause were the essence of the eternal, then the effect would also be eternal, which is absurd. If the cause were a distinct meaning, then it would have to subsist, which is impossible for an attribute of an eternal being, and so on. Thus the argument of the eternalists is refuted, and the passage concludes that their claim that the world cannot be originated is false. Note: This refutation is presented within the context of the eternalists' own assumptions.

Key Terms
إيجاد (ījād)
موجد (mūjid)
شبهة (shubha)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the eternalists' argument is logically sound and proves the world is eternal.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that the eternalists' own reasoning leads to contradictions, thus their argument is unsound and does not prove the world's eternity.

Checkpoint
In the refutation, what is the consequence if the bringing into existence (ījād) were the essence of the eternal bringer into existence?
A) The world would be eternal.
B) The bringing into existence would be originated.
C) The eternal would be dependent on the world.
D) The world would have no existence.

Key Takeaway
The eternalists' own logic refutes their position, affirming the createdness of the world.

?
The eternalists relied on their own reasoning to deny a clear truth. Where in your life have you trusted your intellect over divine revelation, and what would it cost you to submit that intellect fully to God?
?
This passage shows that even sophisticated arguments can be false. Which comfortable belief or assumption about God or the universe are you clinging to that this lesson challenges, and what is one concrete step you will take today to align your belief with truth?

Refuting the Annihilation Argument for Eternity
Refutes the annihilation argument for the world's eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Kalām refutation of the claim that the world's possible annihilation implies its eternity, through a logical analysis of the modalities of annihilation.

Core Idea
Annihilation cannot prove eternity; it leads to contradictions.

This passage continues the refutation of those who argue for the world's eternity by pointing to its possible annihilation. The author, al-Nasafi, presents a logical dilemma: either the act of annihilation is a separate meaning from God or it is identical with the world. He then examines each possibility to show that both lead to absurdity, thereby undermining the argument for eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'either...or...' This will help you follow the logical argument.
2
Identify the main dichotomy
The passage presents a dichotomy about the nature of annihilation (iʿdām). Identify the two possibilities and write them down. This is the core of the refutation.
The dichotomy is: either annihilation is a separate meaning from God, or it is identical with the world.
3
Analyze the first branch: eternal annihilation
Examine the argument that if annihilation were eternal, it would necessitate the world's non-existence from the beginning. Consider how this contradicts our observation of the world's existence.
The author uses sense (ḥiss) and evidence (bayān) to reject this.
4
Analyze the second branch: originated annihilation
Now examine the other possibility: if annihilation is originated, then it must be either identical with the world or something else. The argument shows that if it is something else, it cannot annihilate the entire world because the annihilation itself would be an originated thing.
The passage cuts off but the logical point is about infinite regress or contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا لو جاز عدم العالم إما أن كان إعدام القديم إياه معنى غيره من أجل وجوده ينعدم، وإما أن كان إعدام العالم هو هو، فإن كان غيره، فإن كان الإعدام قديما فيقتضي انعدامه من الأصل ولا يتصور وجوده، وحين رأيناه موجودا كان الحس والبيان مبطلا كون الإعدام قديما، وإن كان الإعدام حديثا إما أن كان هو عين العالم، وإما أن كان غيره فإذا فسد أن يعدم جميع العالم حتى لا يبقى حادث إذ هذا الذي به عدم العالم حادث
And likewise, if the non-existence (ʿadam) of the world is possible, then either the annihilation (iʿdām) of the Eternal (al-Qadīm) for it is a meaning other than it, because of which its existence becomes non-existent, or the annihilation of the world is itself it. If it is other than it, then if the annihilation is eternal (qadīm), it necessitates its non-existence from the very beginning and its existence would not be conceivable. But when we see it existing, sense and evidence invalidate the annihilation being eternal. And if the annihilation is originated (ḥādith), then either it is identical with the world, or it is other than it. If so, it becomes impossible that all of the world be annihilated such that no originated thing remains, because that by which the world is annihilated is itself originated.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of the absolute eternalists by addressing a second possible argument from their side: the impossibility of the world's annihilation. The argument suggests that if the world could cease to exist, then its annihilation would have to be either a separate act from God or identical with the world itself. The author examines both possibilities. If the annihilation is a separate meaning from the Eternal, then if it were eternal, it would entail that the world never existed at all, which contradicts our observation. If it is originated, then it must be either the same as the world or something else. If it is something else, then the annihilation itself would be an originated thing, and an originated thing cannot annihilate all of the world because that would leave no originated thing to perform the annihilation? The text is cut off but the logical point is that the annihilation itself would be a created event, leading to an infinite regress or contradiction. This refutation shows that the eternalists cannot consistently explain how the world could cease to exist.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the world can be annihilated, it must be eternal because annihilation is a property of eternal things.
Correction: The passage shows that analyzing the nature of annihilation leads to contradictions, disproving that claim.

Checkpoint

A) The world would exist forever.
B) The world would have never existed.
C) The annihilation would be the same as the world.
D) The annihilation would be originated.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of the world's annihilation does not prove its eternity; rather, the logical analysis of annihilation leads to contradictions that undermine the argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Reflect: In your daily life, do you live as if this world is eternal or as if it is temporary and will be annihilated? Now think about your own existence: Are you living as if your own existence is self-sufficient or do you recognize your total dependence on God? Proof of the Temporality of Accidents
Nasafi demonstrates the temporality of accidents by empirical observation and rational necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses the observation of change in accidents and the tripartite division of existence (necessary, possible, impossible) to prove that accidents are temporally originated.

Core Idea
What admits change cannot be eternal; only the necessary of existence endures.

Nasafi begins his proof for the temporal origination of the world by focusing on accidents. He points to a simple empirical observation: a body that was at rest then moves. Both rest and motion are accidents, and their succession shows that each comes into existence after non-existence. This leads to a deeper rational argument: anything that ceases to exist cannot be eternal, for the eternal must be necessary of existence. Understanding this tripartite classification of conceivable entities is essential for the entire proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the text moves from an empirical observation to a rational deduction using the categories of necessary, possible, and impossible existence.
Pay attention to the phrase 'رأينا ساكنا تحرك بعد سكونه' — it anchors the argument in sense experience.
2
Identify the empirical observation
Find the part where Nasafi describes a stationary body moving. He argues that motion came into existence after not existing (hence it is temporal), and rest ceased to exist when motion occurred (hence it too is temporal).
This step shows that the argument is not purely abstract but grounded in observable change.
3
Understand the tripartite division
The text then explains that the eternal must be necessary of existence. It divides everything conceivable into three categories: necessary of existence, possible of existence, and impossible of existence. The eternal cannot be impossible (since it exists) nor possible (since what is possible requires a specifier and is thus temporal). Therefore, the eternal is necessary.
This is a foundational classification in Islamic theology (kalam).
4
Apply the principle to accidents
Since accidents undergo change and cease to exist, they cannot be eternal. They fall under the category of possible existence, which requires an external specifier and is therefore temporally originated.
The conclusion is that all accidents are temporally originated (haditha).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۲ – [إثبات حدوث العالم بجميع أجزائه]:
وبعد وقوفنا على جميع أجزاء العالم، وعلى أقاويل القائلين بقدم العالم أو قدم شيء من أجزائه، يجب أن نصرف العناية إلى إثبات حدوث العالم بجميع أجزائه فنقول: إنا أثبتنا بالدليل أن من أجزاء العالم ما هو عرض ومنه ما هو عين، ليس وراء هذين القسمين شيء آخر من أجزاء العالم، فيجب أن نبحث عن كل واحد من القسمين أقديم هو أم حادث فبدأنا بالأعراض فتأملنا فيها فرأيناها محدثة، وذلك لأنا رأينا ساكنا تحرك بعد سكونه وقد أقمنا الدلالة على كون الحركة والسكون عرضين، فكان السكون قائما بالجسم حين كان ساكنا وقد حدثت فيه الحركة، بعدما صار متحركا، والحركة لم تكن موجودة حال كون الجسم ساكنا فحدثت الآن، فعلمنا حدوثها بالحس والمشاهدة، والسكون كان موجودا وقد انعدم، حيث حدثت الحركة، فعلم أنه كان محدثا حيث قبل العدم لأن القديم مما يستحيل عليه العدم، وهذا لأن القديم ينبغي أن يكون واجب الوجود لأنه لو لم يكن واجب الوجود لكان جائز الوجود أو ممتنع الوجود إذ لا قسمة لما يخطر بالبال ثبوته وراء هذه الأقسام، أعني أنه إما أن يكون واجب الوجود، وإما أن يكون جائز الوجود وإما أن يكون ممتنع الوجود، وبطل كون القديم ممتنع الوجود لأن وجوده قد تحقق، ومحال تحقق وجود ما هو ممتنع الوجود لما فيه من اجتماع الجواز والامتناع، وليس بجائز أيضا أن يقال إنه جائز الوجود، لأنه لو كان جائزا الوجود لكان جائزا العدم لاستحالة القول بكون ما يمتنع عدمه جائز الوجود بل هو واجب الوجود ضروري، فلو كان جائزا الوجود لكان جائزا العدم، وإذا كان الوجود والعدم كل واحد منهما في حيز الجواز لم يختص أحدهما بالتحقق إلا بتخصيص مخصص، وما كان وجوده بتخصيص مخصص كان محدثا، إذ المحدث هو الذي يتعلق وجوده بإيجاد غيره، فأما القديم فمستغن في وجوده عن غيره وإذا ثبت أنه
2. On Establishing the Temporal Origination of the World in all its Parts:
And after we have surveyed all parts of the world and the sayings of those who affirm the eternity of the world or the eternity of some of its parts, we must direct our attention to establishing the temporal origination of the world in all its parts. We say: We have established by proof that among the parts of the world there are accidents and there are substances; there is no other part of the world beyond these two divisions. So we must examine each of the two divisions: whether it is eternal or temporal. So we began with accidents and reflected on them and saw them to be temporally originated. That is because we saw a stationary body move after its rest, and we have established the proof that motion and rest are accidents. Rest was subsisting in the body when it was stationary, and motion occurred in it after it became moving, and motion was not existing while the body was stationary, so it came to existence now, so we know its temporality by sense and observation. And rest existed and then ceased to exist when motion occurred, so we know that it was temporally originated because it admitted non-existence, because the eternal is impossible to admit non-existence. This is because the eternal must be necessary of existence, for if it were not necessary of existence, it would be possible of existence or impossible of existence, since there is no division for what can be conceived as existent beyond these divisions. That is, it is either necessary of existence, or possible of existence, or impossible of existence. It is invalid for the eternal to be impossible of existence because its existence is realized, and it is impossible for the existence of what is impossible of existence to be realized due to the conjunction of possibility and impossibility. Nor is it permissible to say that it is possible of existence, because if it were possible of existence, it would be possible of non-existence, because it is impossible to say that what is impossible to non-exist is possible of existence. Rather it is necessarily necessary of existence. So if it were possible of existence, it would be possible of non-existence. And when existence and non-existence are each in the realm of possibility, neither one is specified for realization except by the specification of a specifier, and whatever's existence is by the specification of a specifier is temporally originated, for the temporally originated is that whose existence depends on the bringing into existence by another. As for the eternal, it is independent in its existence from others. And when it is established that...
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the temporality of the world, specifically the first step: demonstrating that accidents (أعراض) are temporally originated (حادثة). The author states that the world consists only of substances (أعيان) and accidents. He then examines accidents by observing a body that was at rest and then moves. Motion and rest are known to be accidents. Rest existed while the body was still, then motion occurs; therefore motion came into existence after not existing, proving its temporality by direct observation. Similarly, rest existed and then ceased to exist when motion came, so it accepted non-existence, which is impossible for the eternal (قديم). The author then explains why the eternal cannot accept non-existence: the eternal must be necessary of existence (واجب الوجود). He presents a tripartite division of conceivable entities: necessary, possible, and impossible. The eternal cannot be impossible (since it exists), nor can it be possible (because what is possible could not exist and would need a specifier, making it temporal). Therefore the eternal is necessary. Since accidents change, they are possible and not necessary, so they are temporal. The argument is cut off at the end of the page but establishes that anything that changes cannot be eternal.

حركة

حادث
واجب الوجود
جائز الوجود
ممتنع الوجود
مخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that only motion and rest are temporal, while other accidents might be eternal.
Correction: The argument establishes a general principle: any accident that undergoes change or ceases to exist is temporal. Since all accidents are subject to change, all accidents are temporal.

Checkpoint
Why does the author conclude that rest is temporal (hadith)?
A) Because it existed and then ceased to exist.
B) Because it is a substance.
C) Because it is necessary of existence.
D) Because it is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Accidents are temporal because they change and cease to exist; the eternal must be necessary of existence and cannot undergo change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You observe countless changes every day—your mood, your health, your possessions. If everything that changes is temporal and cannot be eternal, where does that leave your heart's ultimate reliance? Will you anchor your hope in the Eternal who does not change, or in the fleeting?
?
The passage insists that the eternal must be necessary of existence—self-sufficient, needing nothing. Now look at yourself: you need air, food, sleep. You are contingent. Does that humble you? Does it drive you to seek the One who needs nothing, or do you still live as though you are independent?
?
Consider one area of your life where you have invested security—perhaps a relationship, a career, or a skill. If it is subject to change, it is temporal. How does this lesson's proof of the temporality of accidents force you to re-evaluate where you place your ultimate trust? What will you do today to realign your heart?

Empirical Observation of Accidents: Rest and Motion
Nasafi presents an empirical observation to demonstrate the temporality of accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the observation of change (rest to motion) provides direct evidence that accidents are temporal.

Core Idea
Accidents are temporal because we see them change.

Nasafi begins his proof by calling us to reflect on the world around us. The simplest observation of a body at rest that begins to move shows that accidents—like rest and motion—come and go. This lesson walks through that empirical foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage about observing rest and motion
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Let the author's description of the observation sink in.
2
Identify the key terms
Pick out the terms for accident (ʿaraḍ), rest (sukūn), motion (ḥaraka), and temporal (ḥādith). Notice how they are used.
3
Reflect on the role of sense perception
Ask yourself: why is sense perception enough to prove that motion is temporal? The passage answers clearly.
Compare with the rational argument in the next lesson.

واحد من القسمين أقديم هو أم حادث فبدأنا بالأعراض فتأملنا فيها فرأيناها محدثة، وذلك لأنا رأينا ساكنا تحرك بعد سكونه وقد أقمنا الدلالة على كون الحركة والسكون عرضين، فكان السكون قائما بالجسم حين كان ساكنا وقد حدثت فيه الحركة، بعدما صار متحركا، والحركة لم تكن موجودة حال كون الجسم ساكنا فحدثت الآن، فعلمنا حدوثها بالحس والمشاهدة، والسكون كان موجودا وقد انعدم، حيث حدثت الحركة، فعلم أنه كان محدثا حيث قبل العدم لأن القديم مما يستحيل عليه العدم، وهذا لأن القديم ينبغي أن يكون واجب الوجود لأنه لو لم يكن واجب الوجود لكان جائز الوجود أو ممتنع الوجود إذ لا قسمة لما يخطر بالبال ثبوته وراء هذه الأقسام، أعني أنه إما أن يكون واجب الوجود، وإما أن يكون جائز الوجود وإما أن يكون ممتنع الوجود، وبطل كون القديم ممتنع الوجود لأن وجوده قد تحقق، ومحال تحقق وجود ما هو ممتنع الوجود لما فيه من اجتماع الجواز والامتناع، وليس بجائز أيضا أن يقال إنه جائز الوجود، لأنه لو كان جائزا الوجود لكان جائزا العدم لاستحالة القول بكون ما يمتنع عدمه جائز الوجود بل هو واجب الوجود ضروري، فلو كان جائزا الوجود لكان جائز العدم، وإذا كان الوجود والعدم كل واحد منهما في حيز الجواز لم يختص أحدهما بالتحقق إلا بتخصيص مخصص، وما كان وجوده بتخصيص مخصص كان محدثا، إذ المحدث هو الذي يتعلق وجوده بإيجاد غيره، فأما القديم فمستغن في وجوده عن غيره وإذا ثبت أنه ليس بجائز الوجود، وعلم أيضا أنه ليس بجائز العدم علم أنه واجب الوجود، وإذا كان واجب الوجود كان ممتنع العدم، وإذا قبل السكون العدم دل أنه كان ممتنع العدم، وإذا ثبت هذا ثبت أنه كان جائز الوجود لا واجب الوجود، وإذا ثبت أنه كان جائز الوجود دل أن وجوده تعلق بغيره وإذا تعلق وجوده بغيره دل على أنه ليس بقديم وإذا عرف هذا علم كون السكون محدثا بهذا الاستدلال وعلم حدوث الحركة بالحس والمشاهدة، وكذا هذا في جميع الأعراض المتعاقبة.
One of the two divisions: is it eternal or temporal? So we began with accidents and reflected upon them and saw them as temporal. That is because we saw a stationary person move after his stillness, and we have established the evidence that motion and rest are accidents. So rest was subsisting in the body when it was stationary, and motion occurred in it after it became moving, and motion was not existing while the body was stationary, so it occurred now. Thus we know its temporality by sense and observation. Rest was existing and then ceased, when motion occurred, so we know that it was temporal because it accepted non-existence, for the eternal is that for which non-existence is impossible. And this is because the eternal must be necessary of existence, because if it were not necessary of existence, it would be possible of existence or impossible of existence—since there is no division for what occurs to the mind of its affirmation beyond these divisions, namely that it is either necessary of existence, or possible of existence, or impossible of existence. The impossibility of the eternal being impossible of existence is invalidated because its existence is actual, and it is impossible for the existence of what is impossible of existence to be actual, due to the combination of possibility and impossibility in it. Nor is it permissible to say that it is possible of existence, because if it were possible of existence it would be possible of non-existence, due to the impossibility of saying that what is impossible of non-existence is possible of existence. Rather it is necessary of existence necessarily. So if it were possible of existence, it would be possible of non-existence. When existence and non-existence are both in the realm of possibility, one of them is not specified for actualization except by the specification of a specifier. And whatever's existence is by the specification of a specifier is temporal, for the temporal is that whose existence depends on the bringing into existence by another. As for the eternal, it is independent of another in its existence. And when it is established that it is not possible of existence, and also known that it is not possible of non-existence, it is known that it is necessary of existence. And if it is necessary of existence, it is impossible of non-existence. And if rest accepted non-existence, it proves that it was impossible of non-existence. And if this is established, it is established that it was possible of existence, not necessary of existence. And if it is established that it was possible of existence, it proves that its existence depended on another. And if its existence depended on another, it proves that it is not eternal. And if this is known, the temporality of rest is known by this inference, and the temporality of motion is known by sense and observation. And likewise for all successive accidents.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This is a proof of the temporality of accidents (aʻrāḍ) using the concrete example of rest (sukūn) and motion (ḥaraka). The author begins by stating that we examine one of the two categories—eternal or temporal—by starting with accidents. Observing a body that was at rest and then moves, we see that rest ceases and motion begins. Since rest was present and then disappeared, it has accepted non-existence, which is impossible for the eternal. To establish this, the author argues that the eternal must be necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd). He presents a threefold division: necessary, possible, impossible. The eternal cannot be impossible because it exists; it cannot be possible because if it were possible it would need a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) to bring it into existence, which would make it temporal. Therefore the eternal is necessary of existence and consequently impossible of non-existence. Applying this to rest: rest accepted non-existence (it ceased), so it cannot be impossible of non-existence; rather it must be possible, dependent on a specifier, and thus temporal. The text contains a problematic line ('dalla annahu kāna mumtaniʻ al-ʻadam') which likely should read 'dalla annahu laysa bi-mumtaniʻ al-ʻadam' (proves that it is not impossible of non-existence) to fit the logic. The argument concludes that rest is temporal by this inference, while motion's temporality is known directly by sense perception. The same reasoning applies to all successive accidents.

Key Takeaway
Sense observation of change provides direct evidence that accidents are temporal.

Look at your own life: what changes have you experienced today—from hunger to fullness, from energy to fatigue, from joy to sorrow? Do you truly feel that you are a collection of temporal accidents, clinging to the Eternal by a thread? What would change in your heart if you lived with this awareness every moment?
?
You rely on your senses constantly, but this lesson challenges you to use them as a door to certainty about the Creator. When you see change, do you automatically turn your mind to the One who creates change, or do you remain distracted by the change itself? Commit now: the next time you observe a change in your body or surroundings, pause and remind yourself: 'This is temporal, and only Allah is Eternal.' Will you do it?

The Eternal Must Be Necessary of Existence: The Threefold Division
Nasafi uses the threefold modal division to prove that rest (and accidents) are temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that the eternal must be necessary of existence, and why anything that accepts non-existence cannot be eternal.

Core Idea
The eternal cannot be possible; it must be necessary.

After establishing that motion is temporal by observation, Nasafi turns to a rational proof for rest. Since rest ceased to exist, it must be possible of existence, not necessary. This lesson unpacks the crucial threefold division of necessary, possible, and impossible existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage that argues about the eternal and the three modalities
Read the passage carefully. Notice how it uses logic to rule out possibilities.
Watch out for the problematic line; the intended meaning is that rest is not impossible of non-existence.
2
Identify the three categories
Find the terms wājib al-wujūd (necessary), jāʾiz al-wujūd (possible), and mumtaniʿ al-wujūd (impossible). Understand each.
3
Understand why the eternal cannot be possible
Follow the argument: if the eternal were possible, it would need a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) to bring it into existence, making it temporal. Therefore the eternal must be necessary.
4
Apply the reasoning to rest
Since rest accepted non-existence (it ceased), it cannot be necessary; it must be possible, and thus temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Eternal is Necessary by Itself, Not by an Attribute
Nasafi refutes the objection that the eternal could be necessary through an attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the eternal must be necessary of existence by itself, and why attributing necessity to a separate meaning leads to absurdities like infinite regress.

Core Idea
Necessity cannot be borrowed; it must be intrinsic.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi addresses a key objection: could the eternal be necessary not by its own essence but through a separate attribute or meaning? The objector suggests that if that meaning ceases, the eternal could become non-existent. Nasafi refutes this by showing that such a meaning would lead to either infinite regress or make the essence directly necessary, proving that necessity must be intrinsic.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Carefully read the objector's challenge: why can't the eternal be necessary by a meaning, which might cease?
The objection is a typical one in kalam.
2
Identify the two possibilities for the meaning
The response divides the meaning into two categories: necessary or possible. Note how each is handled.
3
Trace the infinite regress argument
If the meaning is necessary, it either is necessary by itself or by another meaning. The second leads to infinite regress, which is invalid.
This is a classic burhān al-tasalsul.
4
Understand why direct necessity is more fitting
If the meaning is necessary by itself, then the original essence should be necessary by itself without an intermediary. If the meaning is possible, it would be temporal and the eternal would still be necessary.
The argument concludes that the eternal must be necessary by itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واجب الوجود لذاته لا لمعنى: فإن قيل هذا الكلام إنما يستقيم أن لو ثبت أن القديم واجب الوجود لذاته، فلم قلتم ذلك، وما أنكرتم أنه واجب الوجود بمعنى، ثم يبطل ذلك المعنى فيخرج من أن يكون واجب الوجود، فيجوز حينئذ عليه العدم؟ قيل له لا يجوز أن يكون واجب الوجود لمعنى لأنه لو كان كذلك لكان ذلك المعنى منقسما إلى واجب الوجود وجائز الوجود، فإن كان واجب الوجود لا يخلو أما أن كان واجب الوجود لذاته وإما أن كان واجب الوجود لمعنى، ثم إن كان كذلك لمعنى، فالكلام في المعنى كذلك إلى أن يتسلسل إلى غير نهاية وذلك باطل، وإن كان المعنى واجب الوجود لذاته، فكان جعل الذات واجب الوجود لذاته أولى، ولو كان ذلك المعنى جائز الوجود لكان جائز العدم وكان محدثا، وكان القديم قبل حدوثه أما جائز الوجود وإما واجب الوجود، ومحال أن يكون جائز الوجود فكان واجب الوجود، ويجب أن يكون
Necessary of existence by itself, not by a meaning. For if it is said: This statement is only valid if it is established that the eternal is necessary of existence by itself. Why do you say that? And what prevents you from saying that it is necessary of existence by a meaning, then that meaning ceases, so it ceases to be necessary of existence, and then non-existence becomes possible for it? It is said to him: It is not permissible that it is necessary of existence by a meaning, because if it were so, that meaning would be divisible into necessary of existence and possible of existence. If it is necessary of existence, it is not free from being either necessary of existence by itself or necessary of existence by a meaning. Then if it is so by a meaning, the discussion about that meaning continues similarly ad infinitum, and that is false. And if that meaning is necessary of existence by itself, then making the essence necessary of existence by itself is more appropriate. And if that meaning were possible of existence, it would be possible of non-existence and would be temporal, and the eternal before its temporality would be either possible of existence or necessary of existence, and it is impossible for it to be possible of existence, so it would be necessary of existence. And it must be
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage concerning the nature of the eternal's necessity. The objector challenges the claim that the eternal is necessary of existence by itself (li-dhātihi), suggesting it might be necessary by a meaning (bi-maʻnan), i.e., through an attribute. If that meaning were to cease, the eternal would no longer be necessary and could become non-existent. The response (qīla lahu) argues that such a scenario is impossible. If the eternal were necessary by a meaning, that meaning itself must be either necessary or possible. If it is necessary, then the meaning itself must be either necessary by itself or by another meaning. If by another meaning, this leads to infinite regress (tasalsul), which is invalid. If the meaning is necessary by itself, then it would be more fitting (awlā) for the original essence to be necessary by itself directly, since the meaning would be an unnecessary intermediary. If the meaning is possible of existence, then it would be possible of non-existence and thus temporal. But then before the meaning came into existence, the eternal would have been either possible or necessary. It cannot have been possible (since that would require a prior cause), so it must have been necessary. Thus the eternal must be necessary by itself. The argument is a classic application of the infinite regress argument (burhān al-tasalsul) to refute the notion of deriving necessity from something other than the essence.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود لذاته
واجب الوجود بمعنى
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's necessity of existence is due to an attribute like power or life.
Correction: This argument shows that such an attribute would itself need to be necessary, leading to regress or making the essence directly necessary.

Checkpoint
What is the main problem with saying the eternal is necessary of existence by a meaning?
A) It makes the meaning temporal
B) It leads to infinite regress or a more direct necessity
C) The meaning cannot be necessary by itself
D) The meaning would be possible

Key Takeaway
The eternal must be necessary of existence by itself, not through any separate attribute or meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You daily treat many things as necessities—your job, your family, your health. This lesson proves that only Allah is necessary by Himself. What is one thing you have been holding onto as if it were necessary for your existence, and how can you release it to Allah today?
?
If you truly believed that Allah's necessity is intrinsic, not dependent on His actions or gifts, would your worship change? Would you still complain when He doesn't give? Sit with that question and write down one specific area where you act as if Allah needs to earn your trust by giving.

5 min
The Eternal Must Be Necessary of Existence
Logical proof that the eternal must be necessary of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that the eternal (al-qadim) must be necessary of existence, and how this is applied to accidents to show their origination.

Core Idea
The eternal cannot be contingent; it must be necessary and impossible of non-existence.

This passage presents a rigorous logical argument showing that if something is eternal, it cannot be merely possible in existence; rather, it must be necessary. The author applies this reasoning to the example of rest (sukun) to prove that it is originated, not eternal. By understanding this proof, you will see how Maturidi theologians established the contingency of the world.

Guided Path
1
Understand the logical categories
Grasp the three categories: necessary existence (wajib al-wujud), possible existence (ja'iz al-wujud), and impossible existence (mumtani' al-wujud). An eternal entity cannot be possible; it must be necessary.
This is foundational to the proof.
2
Follow the argument about rest
The author argues: if rest were eternal, it would be impossible of non-existence. But we observe rest ceasing (when motion occurs), so it cannot be eternal. Therefore rest is originated (muhdath).
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Generalize to all successive accidents
The same inference applies to all accidents that come and go. Their temporality proves they are not eternal, pointing to a necessary being that sustains them.
This links to the proof of God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليس بجائز الوجود، وعلم أيضا أنه ليس بجائز العدم علم أنه واجب الوجود، وإذا كان واجب الوجود كان ممتنع العدم، وإذا قبل السكون العدم دل أنه كان ممتنع العدم، وإذا ثبت هذا ثبت أنه كان جائز الوجود لا واجب الوجود، وإذا ثبت أنه كان جائز الوجود دل أن وجوده تعلق بغيره وإذا تعلق وجوده بغيره دل على أنه ليس بقديم وإذا عرف هذا علم كون السكون محدثا بهذا الاستدلال وعلم حدوث الحركة بالحس والمشاهدة، وكذا هذا في في جميع الأعراض المتعاقبة.
It is not possible of existence, and it is also known that it is not possible of non-existence, so it is known that it is necessary of existence. And if it is necessary of existence, it is impossible of non-existence. And if rest accepts non-existence, it indicates that it was impossible of non-existence. And if this is established, it is established that it was possible of existence, not necessary of existence. And if it is established that it was possible of existence, it indicates that its existence is dependent on another, and if its existence is dependent on another, it indicates that it is not eternal. And if this is known, the rest is known to be originated by this inference, and the occurrence of motion is known by sense and observation. And likewise this applies to all successive accidents.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a logical proof establishing that the eternal must be necessary of existence. The author begins with a conditional: if something is not possible of existence and also not possible of non-existence, then it is necessary of existence. Then he argues that if something is necessary of existence, it is impossible of non-existence. Next, he applies this to the example of rest: if rest accepted non-existence, that would show it was impossible of non-existence, but careful—the text says 'if rest accepts non-existence' and then 'it indicates that it was impossible of non-existence'—this seems to be part of a reductio. Actually the reasoning is: if rest were eternal (i.e., not originated), it would be necessary and thus impossible of non-existence. But we observe that rest can cease (non-existence occurs with motion), so rest cannot be eternal; therefore it is originated. The author concludes that by this inference we know rest is originated, and by sense we know motion is originated. This applies to all successive accidents. The proof hinges on the impossibility of an eternal accident being possible of non-existence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sense perception alone can prove the eternity of God or the world.
Correction: The proof uses logical necessity; sense perception only shows that accidents change, which then leads to the inference that they are not eternal. The eternity of God is established through rational necessity, not direct observation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how do we know that rest is originated?
A) By sense perception alone.
B) By logical inference: if rest were eternal it would be impossible of non-existence, but we see it ceases, so it is originated.
C) Because rest is an accident and all accidents are originated by definition.
D) Because the body is originated.

Key Takeaway
The eternal cannot be contingent; it must be necessary of existence, and this is proven by observing the non-eternity of accidents.

?
The argument shows that whatever is possible of existence depends on another. Examine your own life: what do you rely on that is itself contingent? Does this realization move you to place your ultimate trust solely in the Necessary? What specific attachment can you surrender to Him?
?
The passage proves that the eternal cannot be contingent. Reflect on your belief in Allah's eternality—do you truly internalize that He alone is Necessary, and that nothing else shares that attribute? How does that change your view of worldly possessions and ambitions? What is one thing you can do today to prioritize the Eternal over the temporal?

The Eternal is Necessary by Essence, Not by a Meaning
Refutation of the objection that the eternal is necessary by a meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the eternal (God) must be necessary of existence by itself, not through any external meaning, and how infinite regress and priority arguments are used to prove this.

Core Idea
Necessity cannot come from outside; it must be intrinsic.

This passage presents a crucial objection to the foundational kalam claim that the eternal is necessary of existence by itself. The objector suggests that maybe the eternal derives its necessity from something else. The author refutes this by analyzing the implications: if necessity comes from an external meaning, then either that meaning leads to an impossible infinite regress or it is itself necessary by nature—in which case it is more fitting to attribute necessity directly to the eternal essence. This argument solidifies the unique status of God as self-necessitating.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the objector's challenge: could the eternal be necessary by an external meaning that might cease?
2
Follow the two-part answer
The author responds by dividing the possible status of the external meaning into two cases: if it is necessary by a meaning (leads to regress) or by itself (then the essence is more deserving). Follow the logic for each branch.
Pay attention to the term 'tasalsul' (infinite regress) which is deemed false.
3
Reflect on the impossibility of a possible meaning
Consider the third scenario: if the meaning is possible of existence, it would be originated, contradicting the eternal's nature. This forces the conclusion that the eternal must be necessary by itself.
4
Connect to the broader kalam framework
Understand that this argument establishes God's necessity as intrinsic, which is essential for further arguments about attributes and actions.
This is a classic example of using reductio ad absurdum (to infinite regress or contradiction).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل هذا الكلام إنما يستقيم أن لو ثبت أن القديم واجب الوجود لذاته، فلم قلتم ذلك ، وما أنكرتم أنه واجب الوجود بمعنى، ثم يبطل ذلك المعنى فيخرج من أن يكون واجب الوجود، فيجوز حينئذ عليه العدم؟ قيل له لا يجوز أن يكون واجب الوجود لمعنى لأنه لو كان كذلك لكان ذلك المعنى منقسما إلى واجب الوجود وجائز الوجود، فإن كان واجب الوجود لا يخلو أما أن كان واجب الوجود لذاته وإما أن كان واجب الوجود لمعنى، ثم إن كان كذلك لمعنى، فالكلام في المعنى كذلك إلى أن يتسلسل إلى غير نهاية وذا باطل وإن كان المعنى واجب الوجود لذاته، فكان جعل الذات واجب الوجود لذاته أولى ولو كان ذلك المعنى جائز الوجود لكان جائز العدم وكان محدثا ، وكان القديم قبل حدوثه أما جائز الوجود وإما واجب الوجود ، ومحال أن يكون جائز الوجود فكان واجب الوجود، ويجب أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته لا لمعنى لما مر في المعنى الأول، والله الموفق.
If it is said: This statement is sound only if it is established that the eternal is necessary of existence by itself. Why do you say that? And what prevents you from [saying] that it is necessary of existence by a meaning, and then that meaning ceases to exist, so it comes out of being necessary of existence, and then non-existence becomes possible for it? He replied: It is not permissible for it to be necessary of existence by a meaning, because if it were so, that meaning would be divisible into necessary of existence and possible of existence. For what is necessary of existence is either necessary of existence by itself or necessary of existence by a meaning. Then if it is so by a meaning, the discussion about the meaning is likewise, until it leads to an infinite regress, and that is false. And if the meaning is necessary of existence by itself, then making the essence itself necessary of existence by itself is more appropriate. And if that meaning were possible of existence, it would be possible of non-existence and would be originated, and before its occurrence the eternal would be either possible of existence or necessary of existence. It is impossible for it to be possible of existence, so it would be necessary of existence, and it must be necessary of existence by itself, not by a meaning, due to what has passed in the first meaning. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector claims that the eternal might be necessary of existence through an external meaning (i.e., a cause) rather than by itself. If that meaning could cease, the eternal would become contingent. The author answers by analyzing the meaning: if it is necessary by a meaning, that meaning must itself be either necessary by itself or by another meaning, leading to an infinite regress (which is impossible). If the meaning is necessary by itself, it is more fitting to attribute necessity to the eternal essence directly. If the meaning is possible, it would be originated, and before its existence the eternal would have been either possible or necessary—but possible is impossible for the eternal, so it must be necessary. The only way to avoid contradiction is for the eternal to be necessary by itself. The argument uses the principle of infinite regress (tasalsul) and the impossibility of dependence on a contingent cause for the eternal.

واجب الوجود بمعنى
تسلسل

محدث
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's necessity could be derived from something else (e.g., a cause or attribute) such that if that cause ceased, God could become contingent.
Correction: The argument shows that if necessity came from an external meaning, that meaning would itself need to be necessary or possible, leading to either infinite regress or a more direct attribution, ultimately confirming that necessity must be intrinsic.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author gives for rejecting the idea that the eternal is necessary of existence by a meaning?
A) The meaning would be impossible to conceive.
B) It would lead to an infinite regress or force the meaning to be self-necessary, which is less fitting.
C) The meaning cannot be eternal itself.
D) The meaning would make the eternal contingent.

Key Takeaway
The eternal (God) must be necessary of existence by its own essence, because any external cause of necessity would lead to an infinite regress or be superfluous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself thinking that God's love or power over your life depends on your performance or circumstances, as if His necessity could be weakened? How does this truth that God is necessary by Himself—not by any external meaning—challenge that subtle idolatry in your heart?
?
If God's existence does not depend on your faith, your prayers, or your obedience, then why do you often treat your spiritual failures as if they can diminish His reality? Will you meditate on the fact that your sin has no power over His necessity, and let that drive you to repentance rather than despair?
?
The infinite regress argument shows that any chain of dependence must end in a Self-Sufficient Being. In your life, what are the things you rely on as if they could stand on their own? How can you realign your trust to anchor only in the One who is necessary by essence?
